BUCKING-HAM PARK ESTATE,
MEDLOW (Blue Mountains).
This charming Estate has its Railway Platform almost
adjoining, and its level land proves it to be eminently
adapted for residential purposes. Nature seems to have
lavished all her varied charms on BUCKINGHAM PARK,
"Inch is, without exception, the CREAM or PICKED
SPOT on the BLUE MOUNTAINS.
BUCKINGHAM PARK ESTATE
Is beautifully surveyed. The Allotments are unusually
large— from 100 up to 180 feet frontages, with long depths
—thus enabling buyers to have Garden, Tennis Lawns,
etc., if they so desire. In fact, it may be truly said, there
is no finer Residential Land on the Mountains.
. The BELGRAVIA HOTEL, MEDLOW, which cost £4OOO,
is now open for visitors, and is the First Hotel on the
Mountains supplied with the Electric Light, and the
Proprietor is willing to supply any cottages built on the
estate with the electric light. Two elegant Villas are
now nearly finished on this estate. The rapid advance in
the price of land at BLACKHEATH during the past three
years, amounting to fully 300 per cent., clearly shows that
early buyers at Buckingham Park will make a splendid,
investment. Title is TORRENS, and the terms are liberal
and to suit buyers. For particulars, apply to the Pro-
prietor,

SATURDAY, AUGUST 1, 1891.
An Imperial Satrap.
When the first three Guelphs reigned over
Great Britain, the market for first-class
weight-carrying literary hacks was exceed-
ingly good. It was evidently a maxim with
Ministers, in those days, that Truth was
mighty and must prevail—unless a Lie were
allowed a good start. Consequently, when-
ever any big event was on, in which it was
undesirable that Truth should come in ahead,
expert literary jockeys were hired to hustle
along some rattling speedy Lies, and useful
hacks were employed to jostle Truth out of
the running. Latterly this system has
fallen out of vogue. The influence of the
newspaper iiress has overwhelmed the
pamphleteer. But apparently the people of
Australia are to be treated to a revival of the
good old system. Some time ago, Sir
Henry Parkes announced that, inasmuch
as it was most important that every citizen
should be enabled to make himself familiar
with the Federation Bill of the Convention,
he would cause twenty thousand copies of
that measure to be printed and distributed
broadcast. To this proposal no sensible man
could object. But when the actual publica-
tion made its appearance, it was discovered
to have taken an extraordinary form. From
the New South Wales Government Printing
Office was issued a pamphlet which did
indeed contain the Bill, bub with the text so
inextricably mixed up with a pamphleteer’s
gloss or commentary, that it required a
practised reader and critic to distinguish
which part is Bill and which is commentary.
No person, .however, whose attention has
once been directed to this peculiar sandwich-
ing, can fail to perceive one fact, namely :
that the business of the hired pamphleteer
has been to justify and uphold “every
letter, every comma” of the Draft Bill, and
that lie has not been mightily particular
about his methods.
* * *
No time has been lost in making running.
In the very second clause of the first chapter
occurs a provision which, if read straight,
exposes the Imperialistic tendency of the
whole Bill—
2. The Queen may, from time to time, appoint a
Governor-General, who shall be Her Majesty’s Representa-
tive in the Commonwealth, and who shall have and may
exercise in the Commonwealth durinr the Queen’s
pleasure, and subject to the provision of this Constitu-
tion, such powers and functions as the Queen may think
lit to assign to him.
Straightway the pamphleteer begins to ex-
plain copiously. “Exception has been
taken,” he remarks, “to this clause and
others referring to the Governor-General, on
the grounds that they propose to confer
* enormous’ powers on her Majesty’s repre-
sentative, such powers, indeed, as have
never been granted to any Governor in these
colonies.” The pamphleteer is surprised at
people’s stupidity in raising such complaints.
He disposes of them with graceful, and
slightly supercilious, ease. “This conten-
tion,” he airily remarks, “overlooks the fact
that, under parliamentary government, the
power nominally placed in the Governor’s
hands is really granted to the people; because
it can only be exercised by him through his
Executive, who are virtually appointed by the
electors. It follows that, by increasing the
range of power to be vested in the Governor-
General, the measure of constitutional free-
dom to be enjoyed by the people would be
proportionately enlarged.” It follows, like-
wise, that the more power you give the
Governor-General, the less he possesses, and
that if you only conferred a Dictatorship on
him, he would have to ask leave to pick his
teetli. But it doesn’t really matter what
follows The explanation which leads
happens to be dead lame. The Executive
are not “ virtually appointed by the people.”
They are not appointed by the people, either
“virtually” or vice-ually. They are to be
appointed by the Governor himself, and to
be dismissed whenever he chooses ! Here is
the clause : chap. 11. cl. 2 :
There shall be a Council to aid and advise the
Governor-General in the government of the Common-
wealth, and such Council shall be styled the Federal
Executive Council; and the persons who are to be Mem-
bers of the Council shall be, from time to time, chosen
and summoned by the Governor-General, and sworn as
Executive Councillors, and shall hold office during his
pleasure.
It is worth noting that there is not, in the
whole Bill, any stipulation requiring that
such persons need possess any particular
qualification. In order to be a member of
the proposed Senate a man must be, at least,
30 years of age, must have resided in the
Commonwealth for five years, must be a born
or naturalized subject of the Queen ; and
must be an elector of some State. For
membership of the House of Representatives
a somewhat similar qualification is required.
But to be appointed a member of the
Executive Council he need have none of
these qualifications. He may be imported
for the purpose. The Governor-General
may make up his Executive Council of
dudes sent to him for the purpose by the
Secretary of State for the Colonies. He
might place in it the British Admiral and
every one of the captains of the ships-of-war
on the Australian station.
* # *
But this, it may be urged, could never
be conceivable under what the pamphle-
teer terms “ Parliamentary Government.”
Ministers at least would be responsible to
the Legislature and “ virtually appointed by
the electors.” Not at all. Even under the
existing Constitution Ministers need not be
responsible to Parliament or “virtually
appointed by the electors.” At this very
time in N.S. Wales, Postmaster-General
O’Connor, rejected by two constituencies in
succession, is not responsible to Parliament
at all. And so it is in this new proposed
constitution for the Commonwealth. The
Governor is to appoint officers to administer
various Departments who shall hold office
during his pleasure, shall be members of the
Executive Council, and may (or may not)
hold seats in Parliament if they can get
elected. Thus, then, it is demonstrated
that not one single individual to be invested
with the powers and influence of Executive
station is required to be within the control
of the people or the Parliament of Australia!
# * *
Another obvious objection to the wording
of the clause first quoted in this article,
namely, that it embodies in the Statute
Law of the Commonwealth the Queen’s
Letters Patent and Instructions to
Governors-General, is scouted by Parkes’
pamphleteer. Such has always been the
case, he affirms, with questionable accuracy.
Besides, every power and function conferred
on the Governor-General is to be “subject to
the provisions of this Constitution.” Now,
supposing it to be true that an Australian
Governor’s instructions are equal to Statute
Law, that is assuredly a position never
realised by our people. A discovery that
such is the case would be a powerful motive
for a determined effort to sweep away so
offensive an anomaly. It cannot without
effrontery be alleged as a reason for extend-
ing and consolidating the grievance. As to
the second idea, that all powers conferred on
the Governor-General are “subject to the
provisions of this Constitution,” it is an
assumption of the most icy coolness, to put
it forward as granted that the provisions of
this Constitution furnish adequate limitation,
or any limitation at all, of the Governor-
General’s powers to be so conferred and from
time to time modified or extended.
* * *
When the draft Bill is examined with the
object of ascertaining what provisions it may
contain which would set limits to the exer-
cise of powers conferred upon the Governor-
General by the Imperial authorities, it
becomes apparent that there are not any
provisions of that nature ! This Constitution
imposes no limitations on the Governor-
General’s powers, and consequently that is
merely a delusive phrase which sets forth
that his powers shall be subject to its provi-
sions. There are numerous clauses which
confer powers on the Governor-General ;
none which confine his powers. Thus, the
first clause of chapter 11. enacts that “ The
Executive power and authority of the Com-
monwealth is vested in the Queen, and shall
be exercised by the Governor-General as the
Queen’s Representative.” The Parkes
pamphleteer gets up a wrangle with those
critics of this clause avlio have argued that it
ought to have concluded with the words
“acting by and with the advice of the
Executive Council.” Such words, he
affirms, would have been mere surplusage.
This is a sad truth, although in a sense
exactly contrary to the intent of the
hired apologist. Since the Federal Executive
Council is, as we have shown, to consist of
mere nominees and creaturesof the Governor-
General removable at his pleasure, it mat-
ters but little whether he act with their
advice or without it. Should one set of
Executive Councillors prove unmanageable,
all the Governor-General need do is to dis-
miss them and appoint others who would
concur in any course he had predetermined.
Again, it is provided in Clause 3 that “ The
provisions of this Constitution referring to
the Governor-General in Council shall be
construed as referring to the Governor-
General acting with the advice of the Federal
Executive Council.” A couple of piges of
the pamphlet are filled with arguments ex-
plaining that it is of no consequence that in
only two clauses is the term “the Governor-
General in Council ” employed. This is waste
of disputation. Since the “ Executive
Council ” is not a responsible body in any
sense whatever, the liberties of the people
are just as little protected when the Gover-
nor in Council assails them as when the
Governor deals with them on his own
account.
* * *
In this connection it may, however, be
interesting to observe what functions and
authorities are ascribed to “the Governor-
General in Council” and vvhat to “the
Governor-General.” The Governor-General
in Council may (until other provision is made
by the Parliament) appoint and remove all
officers of the Commonwealth other than
Ministers. He may exercise all powers now
vested in the Governor of a colony ; he may
appoint Judges of the Commonwealth Courts.
The Governor-General, independently of the
Executive Councils, is Commander-in-Chief
of the Military and Naval Forces of the Com-
monwealth. He may return to Parliamentany
law for such amendment as he may indicate ;
he may appoint and dismiss Ministers ; he
may depute his powers ; he may assent to,
decline, or reserve for Queen’s pleasure any
law ; he may fix the times for assembling
Parliament, may prorogue it, and may dis-
solve the House of Representatives. He
may recommend appropriations of money,
and no appropriation can be made by Parlia-
ment unless he shall first have recommended
same. And, finally, he can, as has been
already shown, exercise any other powers or
functions which the Queen may, from time to
time, confer upon him,
# * *
The foregoing summary must have made
it clear that under the proposed Constitution
for the Commonwealth of Australia, the posi-
tion of the Governor will be that of a despot
restrained only by public opinion. Consti-
tutional check there is none. All the
pamphleteering jargon about Parliamentary
Government being a safeguard, is mere
deception. Over the decrees of the Gover-
nor-General, the personnel of the Federal
Executive Council, and even the acts of the
Ministers of State, the Parliament will have
no control whatever. The chain of respon-
sibility is designedly disconnected where its
strongest shackles should be. Of the two
chambers of the Legislature, the Senate is to
be only indirectly connected with the people.
As for the House of Representatives, that
indeed will be in touch with the electors,
but it may pass resolutions of want of con-
fidence till its members are grey-haired
without compelling the Governor-General
to dismiss a single Minister, if he be deter-
mined to retain his discredited nominees.
A dissolution of the House by effluxion of
time will indeed give the electors an oppor-
tunity of depriving unpopular Ministers of
their seats in that body. But even such an
event would not shift them from their control
of the executive offices of the Commonwealth.
Were a Governor-General resolute to carry
on designs repugnant to Australian aspira-
tions and destructive of Australian
liberties, there would be, under this precious
Bill, absolutely no constitutional method
for controlling or resisting him. He would
not be dependent on Parliament for legal
sanctions to forward his designs, since, as
is admitted by Parkes’ scribe, the Queen’s
letters patent and instructions are to be
equally valid with statute laws.
# * *
But, it will probably be retorted, by
apologists for the Bill, this is much ado about
nothing, seeing that in many particulars the
Governors of Australian colonies are invested
with powers identical with those to which
we have directed attention, and that no
abuse of these powers is on record. This
would be a fair objection, and wo propose to
deal with it. In the first place, it is very
poor philosophy to allege, when one dis-
covers that one has been living over a
volcano, that in building a new house a
crater should be chosen for its site. Although
it was not inexcusable to accept in 1855 a
Constitution in which the power of can-
celling whatever apparent concessions in the
direction of self-government was embodied,
the case is distinctly otherwise in 1891. It is
not now for detached provinces with popula-
tions of a few hundred thousands each that
a Constitution is being framed, but for a
federated continent inhabited by upwards of
four millions of people. The restraints
which were reasonably held in reserve in the
earlier Constitutions become intolerable in
the later one. Leading-strings, which are
appropriate when attached to the toddling
infant, are positively insulting when con-
nected with the vigorous adolescent.
Federated Australia is entitled to demand
that its autonomy shall be real —not sham.
To offer a pretended liberty which conceals
a latent despotism is to affront the independent
spirit of antipodean Britons.
# * *
It is more than probable that were the
Convention Bill to come into operation, its
machinery would begin to revolve with per-
fect smoothness. The Governor-General’s
instructions would be made studiously in-
offensive. He would appoint to the Federal
Executive Council men who had, by re-
peated election to the Colonial Leg'sla-
tures, received the stamp of public confi-
dence. His Ministers would be chosen from
members of the Legislatures just as they are
now ; and, on a vote of want-of-confidence
being carried against them, he would dismiss
them and send for the mover of the resolu-
tion. But in the easily-conceivable event
of projects of vital importance to the
Imperial authorities clashing with interests
of equal importanee-to Australians, the iron
hand would make itself felt under the soft
glove of superficial constitutional practice.
Were it, for example, essential to Aus-
tralians that Mongolians should be rigorously
excluded from the territory of the Common-
wealth, while it was at the same time of
deepest importance to the British Govern-
ment that nothing should occur to incline
the Chinese rulers to cultivate terms of
alliance with Russia, the latent autocracy of
the Governor-General would come into
active exercise. Or were it sought to con-
vert the Commonwealth into a British
recruiting-ground in the teeth of the refusal
of the Legislature to countenance such
a depletion of the industrial population, the
Governor-General would be master of the
situation. Every department of the Execu-
tive would be administered and controlled by
one of his own creatures. Parliament might
protest in vain. The work of enlistment
would proceed under the sanction of the con-
stituted authorities. The police, the jus-
ticiary, the military, the post and telegraph,
the treasury, the ports —even the railways,
would be controlled by persons out of the
reach of Parliamentary influence. The Aus-
tralian nation would be paralysed by the
shocks of the very Constitution it had
accepted. Only a successful revolt against
Authority entrenched in possession of all the
resources of the Commonwealth could extri-
cate the people from the invaders of their
supposed rights and liberties.
# * *
A Bill so framed is an imposition on the
people of Australia. The new Federal Con-
stitution should leave nothing which is
ostensibly conceded, as being established by
precedent and custom, to be read between
its lines. Every right and privilege which
constitutional practice has brought into
exercise should be emphatically asserted in
the text of the Bill. The Federal Constitu-
tion should be the Magna Charta of Austra-
lian rights and liberties, and it should be
made clear that any infraction of that
Charter, whether it were by the British
Sovereign or the British Parliament will
be regarded and resisted as treason against
the Australian nation.
The Colston Case.
What is, or what is not, insanity, according to
the legal signification of the term? At the
trial of William Colston, the Gippsiand
assassin, which concluded the other day in
Melbourne, the legal authorities made about
the greatest effort that has ever yet been put
forth in the law-courts of Australia, to settle
this old, vexed problem, and at the end they
seemed to be further away from a solution than
at the commencement. The criminal code,
being based on alleged Christian principles,
makes no adequate provision for the hanging of
lunatics, and, therefore, when a supposed
lunatic is in the dock on a capital charge, the
local dry bones of medicine and science are
gathered together, and they all grope in the
dark for that definition which never comes.
Some of them, it may be, are assembled in the
cause of humanity; but this seems rather
doubtful, for when the case is one of flogging,
imprisonment, or penal servitude, they are
generally absent, and the law gaily shoves the
prisoner through, be his mental state what it
may. Some, possibly, turn up in the interests
of science ; and the balance are actuated most
likely by that morbid craving after other
people’s corpses, after hearses, funerals, sudden
deaths, and bloated remains that are fished out
of rivers, which seems to be inherent in the
human race at large. But the results are
always very much the same. It is unanimously
agreed that if the prisoner is insane he should
not be hanged, and if he is not insane lieViould
be hanged. Nobody ever succeeds in describing
insanity. The witnesses lose themselves one
after another in a great waste of decom-
posed Latin, with occasional black and age-
blasted pinnacles of Greek standing up out of
the ashes of the other dead language, and swap
many different varieties of ignorance, and that
is about all. They are all struggling to define
the undefinable, and when they have all success-
fully demonstrated that, despite their learning 1
they know nothing whatever of the question, it
is relegated to a chance committee of 12 trades-
men, cheesemongers, builders, and such-like,
And thereupon these individuals rise to the
occasion, and without any knowledge or study
or information whatever, they answer the vexed
question in one act—the hangman helping them.
All the scientific men of the land may struggle
for months with a big problem, and get no
nearer the answer, but a dozen drapers or boot-
makers who want to get home to tea can settle
the same difficulty in less than a dozen minutes.
* * *
The question of insanity assumes tremendous
importance in all cases involving the capital
penalty. If an individual in the earlier and


more obscure stages of mental disease is sen-
tenced to imprisonment or penal servitude, the
chances are that he developes in course of time
into such a lunatic as can he discerned with the
naked eye, and without the aid of any classical
information or old cuneiform knowledge. His
case, in the end, settles itself, but the indi-
vidual who is hanged in the first beginnings of
his complaint is past all such development.
Therefore, when twelve jurymen who know
nothing of insanity, decide offhand, in such an
instance as that of Colston, that the accused is
altogether clothed and in his right mind, it
would be a matter of some interest to find out
if they knew what they were talking about.
When a jury declares that a prisoner is not in-
sane, in the face of a vast mass of tangled
medical testimony, and a Judge passes sentence
on the strength of that decision, it is fair to
assume that they both know what insanity is—
for if not, then they certainly are unable to tell
what is not insanity. And if they do know,
(then it is equally fair to assume that they can
perform amputations successfully, and can open
up the human inside and fool among the vitals
with lancets and other dangerous instruments
with reasonable skill, and that they are able to
distinguish the different varieties of germs, and
are learned in plagues and leprosyies, in
chemistry and botany and mysterious poisons,
that they are profoundly versed in anatomy,
■and that they know a great variety of other
mysteries. For all these things are only the
beginning of medical science, and the in-
scrutable problems of mental disease are the
further end ; and therefore if the jurymen know
nothing of these mere rudiments of knowledge
they know r less than nothing of the question
which they decide so glibly in finding Colston,
and others like him, guilty of wilful murder.
And it is safe to wager a hundred to one that
this jury did know nothing of any of these
things—that hardly one among them knew the
names of his own bones even, or the locality of
his own vitals. Yet when half a dozen
eminent doctors swore positively that the
prisoner was insane, and half a dozen more
swore, though rather less positively, that he
was not, the question was thereby left to
the unaided knowledge of the jurymen almost
as if there had been no medical testimony at all,
an d the 12 butchers and bakers and others rose
cheerfully in their panoply of ignorance, and
showed themselves 20 times more sure because
they knew nothing, than did the men who
knew just enough to know that they didn’t
know everything. If they had been 12 naked
cannibals from the burning Niger bringing in
a solid verdict that there never was and
never could be such a thing as ice, they could
hardly have been more unanimous.
# * *
Probably if they, or any other 12 jurymen,
whose verdict turned entirely on the sanity or
otherwise of the prisoner in the dock, had been
asked to give a meaning to the term madness,
they would have replied in the old, stereotyped,
meaningless phrase, that it was a derangement
of the intelligence. What the intelligence is
neither they nor the Judge nor the prosecuting
counsel could have explained. Presumably
there is such a thing, but as it can only be
judged from outward effects, and as these effects
may possibly be referred to the wrong cause,
there may be no intelligence at all. 1 he old
Spanish Inquisitors knew to a dead certainty
that there was a material devil when they saw
the first rude model of a steam-engine, but it
was proved in later years that a steam-engine
didn’t prove the existence of devil —it only
proved the existence of steam, faking the in-
telligence for granted, however, the derange-
ment simply infers that the mental faculties are
not arranged as they ought to be. And then
the question arises—how ought they to be
arranged? A lunatic is a person whose intel-
lect, in a general and uncertain way, is dif-
ferent from that of the great mass of society, or,
in other words, the intellect of the great mass
of society is different from his. The matter
may be expressed either way. He is commonly
regarded as an individual who has lost some-
thing out of the mental part of him, but what it
is that he has lost, or where it was when he had
it, or how he lost it, are all absolutely unknow-
able. He is judged in a hazy and indefinite
fashion by his actions, but no two medical
authorities are agreed as to what a lunatic
ought to do, and, if they did agree, it is ton
chances to one that he wouldn’t do it. Also,
most of the things that a lunatic is supposed to
do are also done at times by sane members of
society, and the symptoms which he ought to
present are found scattered promiscuously
among philosophers, literary men, and scientists.
The only fact that is absolutely certain is that
insanity exists, but the finer shades of it are
beyond accurate definition by anyone save a
juryman. His idea of a maniac is an individual
who howls in an asylum, and as the prisoner
can’t be in an asylum seeing that lie is in the
dock instead, and as he isn’t allowed to howl,
for the police would stop him in the middle of
his top-note, even if they had to kill him to do
it, the average jui’y never has any difficulty in
corning to a decision. And they always decide
for the hangman. #
The one unique feature about the Colston
case was that there was nothing whatever to
be decided except the abstruse question of the
prisoner’s mental condition. He pleaded
guilty, and acknowledged his crime with
fatuous self-complacency. Therefore, the
twelve bakers and clerks and ironmongers and
were summoned for no other purpose
/save to decide the most fathomless question in
medical science. If the respectable pastrycook
who acted as foreman of this body had been
appointed to amputate a leg, or to form an un-
biassed opinion about the origin of old Egyptian
learning, or to be Government astronomer, the
soul-wrecking folly of the situation would not
have been one-tenth so complete. Apart from
this, however, there was no particular differ-
■ ence between this case and a hundred others.
The jurors were blandly requested to state in
their verdict whether the prisoner knew the
full meaning of his act, just as they might have
been requested to state if the people who lived
before the old red sandstone period ever bought
time-payment furniture ; and they promptly
answered that he did. If they had been asked
instead to decide, out of their own unaided
intelligence, whether John Smith, of 1 hursday
Island, knew the exact date of the fall of
Carchemish they might possibly have hesitated.
There is something stupendous about the spec-
tacle of a presumably sane judge asking a pre-
sumably sane jury to state positively that a
man, whom neither of them ever saw before,
knows a thing which all the world put together
does not know, but the event is of constant
occurrence and excites hardly any comment.
* * *
The science that deals with the mysteries of
the human mind is still in its infancy, and its
professors are only beginning to know the
awful vastness of the problems which they do
not know, and which they have yet to learn.
In the olden days the physician knew every-
thing by reason of his ignorance ; he operated
on the things he understood by means of an
axe and a saw, or with a huge blue pill; and
the things which he did not understand were
disposed of by the hangman on the general
ground that they arose somehow from the devil.
And the modern juryman, taken by chance
from the partially-educated masses, and set
down to give a brief and definite answer to the
question which is as yet unanswerable, is in
exactly the same situation as the brutal and
ignorant charlatans, witch-finders, or exter-
minators of heretics in the past. He thinks
that he is learned because he lives in the midst
of learning, but in reality he knows no more
than did many of his aboriginal ancestors. He
is supposed to be enlightened because he was
born in the age of steam and iron, of telegraphs
and telephones, of gunpowder and strange
explosives, of astronomical discoveries, and
great chemical experiments, and sanitary science
and abundant literature ; but in most cases he
merely resides among those things as a cow
might do, understanding no more about their
methods and causes than did the bigots who
used to burn inventors for being in league with
the Powers of Darkness. He has grown accus-
tomed to wonders and is too inert for wonder-
ment, and therein lies all the difference. And
because he knows nothing of the things which
can be learned by any person of ordinary intelli-
gence, he undertakes to know by instinct the
things which no learning has ever yet fathomed,
and to hang other men by virtue of his know-
ledge. Mental science as yet is practically non-
existent. Such arts as phrenology, hypnotism,
clairvoyance and thought - transference, are
merely the first beginnings of an effort to pry
into the mystery of human intelligence, and it
is safe to aver that the ordinary citizen knows
nothing even of them. They would be of little
use to him if he did, except to reveal to him
the vastness of his own ignorance ; but he
doesn’t want to know his ignorance. It is
simpler and more expeditious to asphyxiate the
unsolvable problem than to investigate it, and,
besides, he isn’t even aware that there is a
problem.
• * *
Such a case as that of Colston is an un-
answerable argument against capital punish-
ment. When a dozen able physicians, after six
months’ study of the prisoner’s mental condi-
tion, were utterly unable to decide whether he
was or was not a responsible being, it is nothing
but hard lying to profess that a dozen casual
citizens, fished at random out of shops and
bakehouses, could settle the problem in three
days’ investigation, and it is nothing else than
murder to execute any individual by reason of
their decision. Yet in every case, which hinges
on a question of sanity, the decision is arrived
at in precisely the same fashion. Colston may
be a murderer in the legal acceptation of the
term, but it is beyond human possibility that
the jury could know whether he is or is not.
There is abundant reason to keep such an indi-
vidual in life-long confinement, because if he is
not insane he is an assassin, and if he is not an
assassin then he is too dangerous to be at large.
But the mistakes made by the gallows are
hopeless and irreparable, and no court has a
right to do an act which admits of no revoca-
tion, unless error is absolutely impossible.
And when the tribunal which decides the
question of life and death is avowedly as
ignorant of the subject under discussion as a
jury of monkeys would be concerning a problem
of the binomial theorem, the farce becomes too
pitiable for expression.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Jury-box Logic.
Any casual reader of this paragraph may be
hanged some day—to be more explicit, he may
be hanged any day. Therefore, he may take a
certain interest in knowing how and why he
is hanged, and by what means a presumably in-
telligent jury arrives at its conclusions. In the
case of William Colston, sentenced the other
day in Melbourne, despite the fact that various
eminent doctors swore positively to his insanity,
it transpires that one juryman out of the twelve
refused to commit himself to the murder ver-
dict. He knew nothing of complex mental
diseases, and being utterly unable to tell
whether the prisoner was crazy or not he had a
certain reluctance to hang him on account of a
juryman’s ignorance. The foreman, however,
won this dubious individual over, and being in-
terviewed in his bakehouse by a Standard
reporter, he told with asinine self-complacency
how he did it: —
“ Look here,” I said to him, “you may go away from
this place to-night, and you will take with you the fees
which a grateful country and the sheriff have given you
for your services. A big larrikin may be waiting for you
round the corner. He knows what money you have, and
is determined to have it. He knocks you down, kicks
you nearly to death, and robs you. He does everything
but kill you. He may be brought into court next week
and charged with the crime. These doctors—or some
others -may come and swear that the prisoner has
general paralysis of the something or the other, and that
he is not responsible for what he has done. The jury
believe them, and the ruffian goes scot free, while you go
to the hospital for a few months, permanently injured,
perhaps. It will be a great consolation to you and to
society to know that the man is mad, and must not be
punished.” This was the clincher, the obstinate juror
could hold out no longer ; all hands agreed that it would
be a bad precedent to give ear to such evidence.
Now this was the exact reason why the one
recalcitrant juror gave in—consequently it is
the one reason why Colston is to be hanged.
And yet the reasoning is too blindly stupid on
the face of it to impose on a black aboriginal.
It assumes, in the first place, that every larrikin
who commits highway robbery is declare*! to be
insane on the same eminent authority as
Colston ; or else, it assumes that if Colston is
saved from the gallows on the testimony of six
medical men, therefore every offender will be
released for ever afterwards without any testi-
mony at all. It is taken for granted, in fact,
that unless Colston is hanged, whether he is
insane or not, therefore all other criminals will
get off, even if their sanity is perfectly obvious.
It is also taken for granted that if Colston had
been found “not guilty” on the ground of
lunacy he would have got away “scot free,”
which is glaring idiocy, for Colston would there
and then have been started away to perpetual
confinement in an asylum. It accepts it as read
that the hypothetical larrikin who might have
kicked the hypothetical juror would want to be
found a lunatic, which is an outrage on common-
sense and an insult to any white man’s intelli-
gence, for he would only make his case worse
by so doing, and escape six months in gaol to
do ten or twenty years in Bedlam. Evidently
the whole reasoning is based on the assumption
that a criminal lunatic is invariably turned
loose on society by reason of his very lunacy.
Further, it is assumed, as a sort of side issue,
that the hanging of one man, whether done
justly or unjustly, will discourage another man
from committing assault and battery, which is
as reasonable as the theory that the kicking of
a dog to-day will make a cow behave itself next
week. In fact, there are about six inferences
in the one argument, and every one of them is
too foolish to pass muster in-a kindergarten.
But this soul-destroying ignorance fetched the
jury in one act. If The Bulletin ever gets
into a tight place it will strike for a jury of
Chinese with an aboriginal foreman. The white
variety is no good.
♦
No Use.
The other day* the false prophet at the other
end of the cable wired out the surprising state-
ment that Wales has a soul above baccarat and
horse-racing, and that “he is desirous of
having serious work entrusted to him,” though
unhappily he can’t get it. The assertion in
itself hardly worth noticing, only by virtue
of their own inherent stupidity sundry loyal
papers have been gulled into taking it up, and
preaching a sad and soulful homily upon it.
The burden of their outpouring was just this ;
the country won’t let Wales work, and then it
slings language at him because he doesn’t work.
He can’t be a practical soldier, for he hasn’t any
military capacity, and it would be suicide to
entrust him with an army. If he goes in for
being a radical reformer he will raise Cain
among the worshippers of Property, and will
undermine the throne. If he takes violently to
religion he must either support one existing
sect and aggravate the rest, or else he must
start anew sect and aggravate the wdiole lot, and
that will also gnaw away the pillars of royalty.
He can’t be allowed to take a hand in politics,
for though he has a seat in the House of Lords,
he is only tolerated there, and the nation would
not stand royal interference in legislation.
Literature isn’t in his line, neither is Art; he
couldn’t even grind out a Journal in the
Highlands, and the horrid daubs which were
perpetrated by his father are quite beyond his
scone. If he ever had any musical taste it has
been spoilt by too much National Anthem; and,
therefore, to sum the whole situation up there
is nothing he can do but smoke and play cards,
and look out with a dull, dead intensity from
the window of a club-house, and be bald and
weary, and wish that he were dead. And just
here the question arises—what in thunder is
the good of having him at all? The whole
argument is based on the fact that there are a

either. And this is quoted as a solid reason
why the nation should stop complaining. It
looks a great deal more like a reason why the

Late English cablegrams tell us how the Cold-
stream Guards have mutinied in a body and
have forced their decision down the throats of
the authorities. This reminds us that Dilke
the other day described the British military
system as one of waste, tempered by imbe-
cility, and scathingly satirised the army’s
total disorganisation —its deficiency in artillery,
cavalry with 7831 horses to 13,414 men, &c.
He declared that England spends more money
on her land-forces than does either France or
Germany, and nearly treble as much as Russia.
Yet, he continues, “ England can put into the
field only about as many guns as a single French
artillery garrison, such as that of Clermont,
can turnout.” He tells us how little Switzer-
land and Roumania possess each about 300 guns
they “can horse and put in the field,” while
England has 112 only that it could utilise;
how, lately, at Aldershot, “ to equip four guns
for service, three batteries were disintegrated,
all these three batteries being “in the First
Army Corps, which the country is told is ready
to take the field how the coaling stations and
telegraphic centres are virtually undefended ;
how the mobilisation scheme for defending
London with 60,000 volunteers and 150 guns
only exists on paper ; and how, in general, the
British Army is a hollow, cumbrous fraud, the
plaything of girncrack peacocks and a danger-
ous and deceptive weakness to the empire.
When Australian politicians gabble torn my rot
about the colonies being defended by Britain’s
fearful might, shy Dilke at them.

Dr. Morson, who seconded, at the Sydney
meeting of the A.M.P. members, the motion
for the extension of the society’s business to
London, based his action on the proposition
that the association would obtain in England
“picked lives”—to wit, the Peerage. Con-
sidering the admirable prospects before the
Peerage and gilded loaferdom of Britain
generally of enjoying before the lapse of many
years a short shrift and a long rope, if the
English business should be confined to such
lives it will be exceedingly risky. Only a man
whose eyes tire closed with dolltirs could he
Wind to the fact that the “stable” Govern-
ments of the old countries, with all their social
and commercial excrescences, are precisely
those which are in most imminent danger of
tumbling to the ground. Ala Lanterne is
going to be a pretty popular cry in Europe
shortly, and its shouting will not conduce to
increased bonuses.
Hunk of wisdom from the riianifesto of the
new “Victorian National Party ” :
In New South Wales the two great parties were so
evenly balanced that they were now at the mercy of a
third, and what had happened there would most assuredly
happen here, unless those, who had a stake in the country
had means of organisation to stop it.
The obvious moral of this is, that those who
have a stake in the country, and a lot of steak
in the stomach, must squash that sinful party
that lias the other two at its mercy. Only,
which party is to be squashed ? The Protec-
tionists and the Labour-party are so well-
balanced that the Freetraders have them at
their mercy ; and the Labour-party and the
Freetraders are just about equal, so that they
both seem to be at the mercy of the Protec-
tionists ; and the Protectionists and Freetraders
are so nearly level that the Labour-party, in
theory at least, can do what it darned well
likes with them. And the parties with the
steak seem to assume that., only two of these
bodies have any right to exist, but it doesn’t
say which two. Also, it , takes it for granted
that the other one should be squashed, but it
doesn’t explain which one.
Taking in all the seven provinces that make
up Australia, Tasmania and Maoriland, there is
a total population of less than 4,000,000. There
are seven separate Parliaments, making fourteen
Houses of Legislature in all, and when Griffith
has arranged his scheme for granting autonomy
to Northern and Central Queensland there will
be nine Parliaments, and then the Federal
Parliament will make ten, and as each of them
runs on the bi-cameral principle, there will be
twenty separate bodies, all law-making simul-
taneously. There are about 700 assorted legisla-
tors *at present, and when these other Parlia-
ments are fixed up, there will be something like
a thousand ! They are all passing new laws for
at least six months in the year —sometimes for
nine months, and several thousand lawyers and
Judges knock holes in them as fast as they are
made, and the holes have to be patched with
more laws. How many laws there are now
Heaven only knows, but some day there will be
about 40 to each inhabitant. And then the
question arises—How many laws can one human
being obey simultaneously without bursting his
crust in the process ? Also, if 1000 men spend
all their time making laws, and there are only
4.000. inhabitants to keep them, isn’t it
about time these latter were getting some more
inhabitants to keep them, or else that they were
knocking off some of the law-makers ? For it
seems to us, somehow, as if 500 legislators could
grind out enough Acts of Parliament to keep
the present Australian population going, and
that the other 500 are liable to fill the country
with more Acts than it can hold.
A Farmers’ Conference in Melbourne has,
amongst other things, discussed the question of
beer-adulteration. The practical plough-holder
doesn’t go into the science of adulterants, but
gets at the desired result by a simple short-cut
that is keenly convincing. Thus J. M‘Donald,
of Maffra, Vic. :
If we, in this province, according to Hayter, grow only
1,831,132 bushels of barley annually, and there are
20.000. gallons of beer manufactured, then, if malt and
hops only were used, more than double the quantity of
malt and hops grown here would be required to make it.
The inference being that other materials are
used—notably sugar and glucose. M ‘Donald
held that 15,975,5681b5. of sugar were used in
beer-manufacture in Victoria in one year, which
meant a waste of 31,951,1361b5. of malt —ora
loss to the farmers of £159,775 and to revenue
(in difference of duty) of £158,330. In England,
a brewer cannot legally use more than one-third
sugar in beer manufacture. In Australia,
brewers practically do as they like, and the
tanglefoot which the Gumsucker, Cornstalk,
Croweater, and Bananalander swill is often a
mysterious blend of fermented thickening,
cocculus indicus (or picrotoxine, if you want to
seem modernly learned), salt, copperas, opium,
Indian hemp (cannabis Indira — c. sativa),
strychnine, tobacco, darnel seed (lolium), log-
wood. alum, capsicum, ginger, quassia, worm-
wood’ calamus root, carraway and coriander
seeds, sulphate of iron, vitriol, cream of tartar,
carbonate of potash, oyster-shells, hartshorn
shavings, &c., &c. In fact, it is frequently con-
centrated solution of insanity, or murder at
short range. For once, the \ ictorian farmers
business projects promise to work for the
public health.
The Victorian Assembly has just finished
digging the same old hole that it has dug every
session for the last three years, and which it
will probably keep on digging for three years
more, seeing that the Upper House promptly
fills up the orifice every time. This is a light
and airy way of mentioning that it has passed,
for the fourth time, an ancient bill to amalga-
mate the legal professions. It provides, in a
very few words, that a barrister may be a
solicitor if he feels so inclined, and that a
solicitor may also be a barrister if he has any
desire that way. The public is thus to instruct
its own barrister direct, instead of telling its
tale of woe to a solicitor, and then paying him
to tell as much of it as he hasn’t forgotten to
the person in the fluff wig. If the barrister
undertakes a case and doesn t appear, the bill
makes him liable to be sued for any damages
that may arise, and he is also to return the ( fee
—in short, he is no longer to be a common
swindler, getting money under false pretences.
Also, the barrister is allowed to sue his client
if he has done his work, and the client refuses
to pay. The measure looks simple enough to
please anybody, but the Council settles down,
like a hunk of mud, as usual, and blocks the
way. _____________
Dilke, writing in the Forum, says that
Parkes is too “ large ” for N.S. Wales. This only
reminds us that Barnum was too large for America,
and that the Devil was too big for Paradise.

Cold and callous as one becomes a'ter a Bernhardtian
series of those Frenchy- situations' which the clergy adver-
tise so thoroughly, I really must blushingly admit that
iJarm'ont’s “Pauline Blanchard” —an original play,
staged for the very first time—is far too fervently love-
sick for a stolid community which gets tired of a heroine
who doubles up both mentally and physically because her
boy' hasn’t hugged her for a fortnight. The dressing is
all eostumo frocking. In the first scene a peasant’s dress
of blue and white, which revealed the absence of corsets
more than the usual gown draperies do ; scene 2, a bridal
robe ; scone 15, ditto, but as Pauline has just rushed
through a howling tempest, realism demands that the
hem of (hat snowy robe should be dragged a foot in mud
not spick-and-span as she wore it; scenes 5 and G, robe
de mat worn with a linsey-woolsey petticoat. Darmont
made up the untidy-looking lover A la Antony, facially,
and seemed to revel in the tragic business—the curtain
fell on him tearing a pillow to pieces with his hands and
teetli —but then all the characters shriek nnd tear their
hair, more or joss, until you experience a feeling of pro-
found astonishment when you discover that there is only-
one authorised lunatic in the piece. By the’way, Dan
O Connor was present, and there was something soothing
in his gracing a play in which a heroine snips off hubby’s
pate with a sickle.

Jeanne D’Arc, played for the second and last time at
Her Majesty’s, on 27th instant, was quite a different
sample of the Bernhardt, and I can’t say that I prefer it.
Perhaps 1 am unregenerate, but I like Sarah best as a
faulty, full-blooded nineteenth century woman, not as a
fifteenth century saint—and although she is an inspired
Joan, and acts with a sort of holy hush, still it isn’t her
most congenial part to ray idea. The dressing is quasi-
martial, except in Act 1 when a Marguoritish get-up, even
to the hanging plaits of her hair, is sported. The skirt is
of drab woollen looped through a wooden rosary over a
white jupe ; china-blue waist loosely laced over a white
blouse. In the second act the long-footed boots in soft
white kid are nice, and the war-like array generally is
effective if hardly serviceable. A white cloth kilt pinked
at the edge and split up to the waist, back and front
revealing chain armour; white tunic with sleeves figured
in red, metal girdle and hanging dagger. In this act
Iseult (Madame Mea) wears a gorgeous fete gown, the
court-train of figured pale terra-cotta velvet, the bodice
of white embroidered in gold, zone and et ceteras in
glittering paste, the bodice and train edged with white
fnr. Prince Charles, by the way, was exactly like the
Knave of Hearts. In Act S Bernhardt wears the sam e
boots and armour, the front embroidered with a medallion
of a guardian angel flanked by St. Joseph lilies. The
final stake-scene must have surprised every past or present
fire-lighter in the house, for whoever knew a flame to com-
mence on the outside of a pyre ? A soldier waved a torch
and behold the middle of the pile turned red ! Now, one
is trained up (or down) to greater realism at Her
Majesty's.
Bernhardt doesn’t wear corsets - but the ladies of her
Co. (and some of the men) do, aggressively. Madame
Gilbert goes a step beyond, and wears what looks like a
life-buoy round her hips to widen them, but, alas! the
improver goes from Dan to Beorsheba, the drawback of
hip-pads, and when you catch her sideways she looks
something like St. John’s head on a charger. The rest
looks as if cut out of wood, all sharp angles, while the trail
of the stay-'ace is over them all to the utter extinction of
natural curves. Nevertheless, some of the fair onlookers
decide that their figures are more impress''ve than Bern-
hardt’s.
There are two things local actresses may pick up from
the Bernhardt. To keep a little block of moist red paint
in the handkerchief to apply to the lips from time to
time ; and to keep white powder in ihe top of the corsage
to bo smeared over the face in agony-scenes. It is very
easily done. “Ah! despair!” (two hands clasped over
the easily-concealed powder-puff) “My chock turns pale!”
(floured hands to face, which, emerges, of course, as white
as a miller.) The other night, though, Sara B. hit herself
on the powder-puff, and out floated a betraying white
cloud.

Sydney has reason to clap hands over the appoint-
ment of Mons. Wiegand to the position of City organist,
and those people who wanted to offer a thousand a year
to a certain well-advertised generally semi-fuzzy old
gentleman who made the organ sound like a quantity of
tanks falling down a flight of tin steps, ought to pawn
their musical consciences at the first opportunity. Wie-
gand s organ-playing is full of thrill. Certainly there are
one or two notes in the not-yet-warmed-up instrument
which sound like a person sneezing in a fog-horn, but the
grating horrors of the thing have vanished altogether,
and now nearly all the sounds are smooth and pleasing
and full of delicious, almost unearthly, music. The new
organist is an enthusiast— o;;e secs that by his move-
ments as he plays. The cold, upright, statuesque player
may leave no musical T uncrossed, but there is seldom
any soul in his work. Miegand’s tender v ibrating cords
tremble to your very heart.
N.S.W. Pigeon, Canary, and Dog Society’s Show was
officially opened ay Lady Jersey on 23rd inst. accompanied
by scions Beatrice and Arthur, in the presence of an
invited crowd and unlimited champagne. The opening
took place in the committee-room. Lady Jersey (in
King’s crimson braided in black, black visite and bonnet)
made a rather mysterious speech, in which she accused
the lower creation of “making alarming strides,” and
wound up by “looking forward to the fact that people
would soon have to learn the language of animals”—which
I think most intelligent folks grasp already. For instance,
when I strolled among the dogs and a ferocious-looking
brute made a rush at me, I knew at once what he meant-
A pretty part of the opening was the presentation to the
Jersey children of a pair of prize canaries in fanciful
cages ; and altogether the ceremony was an entertaining
one. There were few ladies present. Apropos the
language of animals, I read the other day that an Ameri-
can naturalist, who has taken the trouble to take phono-
grams of th u -ranees of monkeys, is now able to ask a
monkey in its o.vn language whether it wants milk and to
gain a diiect response. The scientist gathers that the
monkey uses one word to express drink of all kinds.
There were nu uheKess exhibits, but though the little
go’.den can nr es and pigeons (.those with a high ruffle of
feathers looking absurdly like women with Medici collars
on—some wemen carry their heads and walk like pigeons,
if you notice wero really beautiful specimens, still all the
interest cent ed as usual round the dogs, which were
very fierce. One tan-coloured savage sprang out and
tried to nip a piece out of everybody who passed his
elevated cage, and there were several thrilling escapes ;
also a thriving dog-fight took place at intervals between
two hounds in adjacent cages and provided a great attrac-
tion. Hearing how they snorted at one another I could
scarcely believe an attendant who said they were as meek
as lambs when they were let off the chain together.
Madame Bernhardt visited the Show on Saturday, and
exhibited her pampered tyke for that afternoon only.
It is interesting to observe how neatly their vice-
regalities offend neither side. Lady Jersey smiles on one
minus George; “George” circulates round the other
minus “ Margaret,” and thus neither aggregate can crow
over the other as far as patronage goes.
There are some people who are never so happy as when
flunkeying, and, of course, the lady-scribe is the most
most willing hand at it. Her latest local flight is to
speak of (say) “ W. Snobbs, Esq., of Snobbsvifle, Potts
Point; and ‘Snobs Park,’ Bowral ’’—which is duly im-
pressive until one drives past “Snobs Park”-a board
cottage in a few acres of uncleared paddock and a visible
clothes-line at the hack. It is one thing to “keep up a
plice in the country,” and quite another to run a
recuperating “wattle and dab” shanty which ten to ens
the owner lets furnished whenever he isn't recovering in it
himself. Not that there are not dozens of mansions with
gilded gates scattered about the country, but the people
who live in them do so all the year round. The gentleman
who is so elaborately billed as “Potts Point and Bowral ”
generally has to takeoff his tall hat to squeeze through
the door of his Arcadian “seaf.” Which lucid explana-
tion makes mo feel quite myself again.
Sydney ’Varsity first annual sports, inaugurated at the
Association Ground on 22nd inst., were largely and pro-
miscuously attended by a crowd of principally school girls
—anomalous-looking “buds” in half-mast frocks and
women’s fashions (some of them wearing long cloaks to
the heel, while the frocks beneath were cut up to the
garter!), carrying on limitless flirtations with pale and
pimply school - boys, being the principal interest on
the lawn. Among the grown up portion one or
two of the costumes were very pleasing. A
pale-grey tweed with a darker line, trimmed
with vest of gold passementerie and fashionable gold
buttons, was neatest and nicest; a pretty girl wore
severely-cut dark-blue, black hat and feathers ; her pretty
companion in pale-grey with frontal border of fringe and
gimp, silver girdle and flat, grey felt hat and feathers.
A little dot of humanity in a terra-cotta greenavvay coat
was quite irresistible, which same cannot be recorded of a
blonde in blue with a waterspout outline who had torn out
her yellow locks at the back into a sort of a frizzled bob-
tail ; nor of another who had courageously slashed a
bright-blue frock with marigold-yellow. As usual, the
majority of people present never glanced at the sports
proper— only now and again there would be a small rush
of excited females against the fence, and then you knew
that a handful of athletes were sprinting after a brooch or
bangle guerdon, and their various “best” girls all
naturally felt a sort of “cupboard” interest in the
first man. By the way, the band-music was very lively—
catchy, modern airs in place of “Maritana” melodies,
which used to be one of the bores of the Association
Ground reunions.

Physicians and pharmacists unanimously
agree that certain medicinal roots and herbs
possess valuable therapeutical properties. By
subjecting their choicest specimens to a patented
process of the proprietors’, Marshall’s Tonic
Nervine is the grand resultant nerve tonic.

the Address-in-Reply was the largest ever taken

Boxing at Liverpool, Slavin, who was
“ off-colour,” ridiculed Mitchell, and the latter, a
notoriously evil-tempered man, thereupon, taking
advantage of Slavin’s condition, floored him
several times, and finally knocked him through
the ropes. Slavin, however, returned to the
attack, but the audience, seeing the unfairness of
the affair, stopped it. Will Mitchell be game to
tackle Slavin now? Probably not; he is too good
a judge.
“ dim " (P. J.) Clark, general manager in
Australia for the Alliance Insurance Co., and
old-time amateur oarsman, is dead, to the great
grief of a thousand sincere friends scattered all
over, and far beyond, the boundaries of this con-
tinent. A large concourse gathered last Sunday
to see the last of one of the m st loveable men ever
known on land or water. Mr. Clark died of
internal cancer, against the agonies of which he
had long vainly battled.
The Parkesian decision to' retain in office
Postmaster-General O’Connor by elevating him to
the nominee N.S.W. Upper House, after he has
been twice rejected by the popular voice, is an
insult to the principle of government by the
people. The fact that there are local precedents
for so noxious a course only goes to prove that the
two political parties who have hitherto controlled
N.S.W. politics turn-about have an equal con-
tempt for the democracy.
Parkes figures most despicably in connec-
tion with the N.S.W. shearing-trouble. One day
last week he scorned the idea of sending 100
armed men to protect a Western woolshed (con-
veniently forgetting that only nine months before
he zealously sent 100 armed men to protect a
Northern coal-mine); and on the very next day
he issued a proclamation designed to cow the
shearers into abject submission to the squatters,
that is, the money-lenders of Sydney. He carried
his shuffling, shambling policy so far as to allow
the proclamation to go forth without his official
signature, hoping thus to curry favour with his
“Labour” masters. The dodge was too trans-
parent to succeed. As Parkes is charged by the
Governor with the custody of the Great Seal of
the province, the Great Seal could not have been
attached to the Parkesian proclamation without
Parkes’ cognizance and consent. He cocked a
deaf ear t > Crick’s denunciation of the illegality of
tlic “ proclamation.”

After the Sydney Choral and Orchestral
Society had performed Gounod’s oratorio of
“ Tobias,” under the leadership of Mens.
Kowalski, at Sydney Centenary Hall last Friday
evening, and a miscellaneous concert, they
adjourned to the inner vestibule of the Australia
to serenade Sara Bernhardt, who, accompanied
by the faithful Seylor, Darmont, and Dr. do
Lambert, delightedly looked down from an upper-
window. The choir first sang Joncicres' “Li
Tsing,” in which Miss Col bourne Baber sang the
solo, and then Deslonis gave vent to “ Les
Rameanx and “La Marseillaise ” with a fire and
power that made the great actress clap her little
hands excitedly.
* * #
During her stay in Sydney Sara has not
disported herself so freely as in ’Frisco, where sha
visited Crernorne, had a slogging-match got up for
her special edification, and actually gave a per-
formance at a Chinese theatre, after drawing 65
cents from the treasury, on account. On this
occasion the orchestra was absent, and Sara
started the show by imitating the wheezy, halting,
jumbled wails of the Chinese instruments.; then
she danced a Chinese cachuca, and concluded the
performance by running amok among the other
performers with the two-handed sword that had
hung from the shoulder of the juvenile lead.
* * #
It is acknowledged by the cognoscenti that
“Pauline Blanchard ” only wants judicious prun-
ing to present one of the finest vehicles for the
display of Sara’s versatility in her possession. One
thine is certain—she has not done better work
than in it since arrival. For the sweet sake of the
author possibly, animated probably by the spice
of novelty, she acted like one possessed.
# * #
The pious and the puritanical continue
to advertise Sara gratuitously by attacking her in
the dailies. Their case is stronger than then-
power to handle it. As someone has put it, Sara
is a fantastic, morbid, artistic eccentricity who
habitually appears in dramas that are mostly
nightmares, and scarcely ever tries to present a
type of woman that can be seen without shame or
remembered without abhorrence. It cannot be
denied that the characters she represents are the
products of an unhealthy social condition and a
lawless, perverse ingenuity; nevertheless, her
charm of voice, of movement, of appearance,
attract the public, and there’s an end on’t. For
instance, her death as Camille; yon know that it
is merely a theatrical trick, nevertheless, when
she suddenly swings round rigidly, held by
Armand at arm’s length, your pulses leap, your
blood runs cold, and yon are carried out of
yourself.

The afternoon was a daisy on which the
Gosford steamed down the harbour to meet J.
L. Sullivan. About 150 souls - sports, journalists,
actors and managers—were on board by invitation
from Duncan B. Harrison, and the “Evangeline”
quartette, Messrs. Ure, Fitzgerald, Brown and
Edwards, sang like seraphim, while a long array
of empty bottles clinked together harmoniously
in her wake and glinted in the sunlight like mile-
stones on the way to Paradise. But Johnel came
not, and so, after steaming in and out the Heads
for hours unavailingly, Duncan 8., unwilling to
close half the theatres in town for the evening,
regretfully said “’Bout ship.” He returned,
however, accompanied by a faithful few, to pick
up the mighty ex-slugger some iiours later.

T. G. Warren’s “ Nita’s First” has been
previously played locally; first at Sydney Royal in
’B5, and then at the Opera House, in ’B7. Only
put up for three nights and short of rehearsal, it
is excellently played, and was received with
yells of laughter and numerous calls on
first night. The management might wisely
leave “ Our Flat ” on tiie shelf ard run
“Nita’s-First-Kiss-in-the-Dark ” to the end of the
■season. The best-drawn characters in the farcical
comedy are the irritable old Fizzleton and his
stupid, soft-hearted wife, who simply goads him
to madness. Royce, as Irascible, annexes most of
the plauditory pastry, and is ably seconded by
Mrs. Hill, Maggie Knight, Mary Kingsley, Hans
Phillips, Lawrence Cautley, and Charlie Westma-
oott; but Edwin Kelly is cast out of his element
as the silly swell, St. George, and as Miss Prim
and Jane the Misses Mowbray and Gallagher are
somewhat overweighted. On Saturday night next
Charles Cartwright and Olga Nethersole open in
“The Profligate,” a beautiful but quiet play by
Pinero. They may revive “ The Idler ” and
“Moths,” and have, among other plays new to
Australia, Sardou’s “ Scrap of Paper,” Grundy’s
“ Village Priest,” “ Dream Faces,” &c. As sup-
port, Messrs. Poulton, Cates, Russell, and others
will augment the present company.

The appearance of “Bridget O’Brien,
Esquire,” at the Adelaide Royal, has had the
effect of nightly crowding the theatre, the business
done being remarkable. Bridget has thoroughly
established herself as a favourite, and coming so
soon after Madame Bernhardt, her French is more
generally understood than it would have been at
any prior period. All the original scenery and
gorgeous dresses are used in the production, and
the piece seems good for a long run. Vincent
West is in charge of the front of the house.
Tommy Hudson continues to gather in the dollars
at Garner’s Rooms. Felix Tanner attempts a
fast of 40 days under the management of Monty
Browne.
* * *
The re opening of Melbourne Royal on
Saturday night was rather a brilliant success until
the curtain rose. Within the short space of three
weeks the old house has renewed its lusty youth.
The stage has been extended some three yards
forward, and the auditorium, newly upholstered
and electric-lighted, is smart from floor to ceiling,
There is, perhaps, more gilt than innocence about
the place, and the tiers are apt to sparkle in one’s
eye ; also, the proscenium would make a suitable
frame for a picture of the Lord Mayor’s Show, and
the act-drop seems too modest for its gaudy
environment -but, taken altogether, the appear-
ance of the interior is highly refreshing and free
from fleas. One alteration only is for the worse.
The dress-circle floor has been raised so near to
the ceiling that a very tall man, attired in a very
high hat, couldn’t jump for joy in the corridor
without damaging his head-gear. This resort of
fashion is likely to be suffocatingly hot in the
summer time.
# * #
The initial performance of “ The Dancing
Girl ” might be summed up by the faculty as a bad
case of general paralysis of the insane. Acting
and stage management have, no doubt, improved
a little by now, but the doom of this entertain-
ment was pronounced by a disgusted audience on
Saturday night. “The DancingGirT” (as designed
by H. A. Jones) is a beautiful harlot, Drusilla
Ives, built on the lines of Miss Formosa. In Lon-
don she lives with an absurd young dock whose
<anded estate seems to consist of one of Gilbert
and Sullivan’s islands, inhabited by a pious fish-
ing population amongst whom the father of Dru-
sifla is pre-eminent. When Drusilla, garbled as
a tame Quakeress, goes down to see the old folks
at home, and tells them what a nice, steady virgin
s he is in London, the dook also arrives, and sits in
the middle of his island while the tenants ask him
to build a breakwater. Presently he returns to
the wicked world, taking with him, as private
secretary, or something, a virtuous Quaker who
has been making love to Drusilla during her
holiday on the island. The dook knows all about
ms girl’s mash, because she told him of it, and so
he encourages the fellow to come dodging round
Brasilia and himself with a faithful groan
Mien they are resuming their sinful career.
Aow, who but an author named Jones could con-
ceive such a character as that? All this time the
dook is going to the dogs in a monotonous manner,
and when he is on the verge of being sold up he
proposes to marry Drusilla—probably, because he
thinks they might pawn the jewellery he has given
her, and retire into private life on the tickets.
Drusilla is not on, however. She is the first
member of the Gaiety Company (must be Gaiety
Company, for there are no “dancing-girls,” so-
called, in any other) that ever refused to become
a duchess when the coronet was chucked at her.
After this, the dook arranges to give a grand final
flare-up in honour of Drusilla, previous to com-
mitting suicide. He gives it. Awful splendid
affair. Gorgeous staircase leading out of the
middle of a drawing-room, and heaps of supers.,
disguised as pillars of sass'.ety, shaking hands
with the dook at the bottom of the stairs. The
ball above (where all the noble guests clan
their hands and shout “Bravo!” when their
cronk hostess dances a new pas de seal) is
disturbed by the arrival of Ives pere. lie dis-
covers that ids daughter is spiritually lost, and
wants_ to take her back to the good old island.
She wisely declines. Then the aged parent curses
her-and departs, soon after which Drusilla has a
fit of remorse, or spasms, and totters off the pre-
mises. That’s the end of Tier, for the evening.
Then the dook (the guests having all cleared out,
with the exception of one lame girl who must be
going to camp in the ball-room) makes a few
remarks to a bottle of poison, and prepares to die.
The lame girl conies down the ridiculous staircase
at this juncture. Her one purpose in the play is
to limp up to the dook at odd times and tell him
that she wishes to “drag him from under the
horses’ hoofs.” She has a soft, sad, sweet expres-
sion, and her whole life is devoted to warning the
dook, and waiting for him to get under those
“hoofs.” At last she has her long-looked-for
opportunity. The bottle is almost at his lips.
In another instant the allegorical steeds will be
over him. She catches hold of the dook’s arm—he
is saved, saved ! The rest is drivel, pointing to the
probability of marriage between the dear dook
and his preserver. That this yarn should have
carried London b} 7 storm can be easily accounted
for. In the first place, dukes and dancing-girls
are indigenous to the soil, and the subject, wildly
ridiculous as it is, has an appearance of local
interest. In the second, but more important
place, the play contains several strong, though
utterly false, situations, to which accomplished
and well-placed artists would be able to give a
veneer of possibility.
* # #
Miss Laura Yilliers has brought over a
very second-rate English provincial company,
utterly incompetent to deal with a play in which
polished acting and a bong-tong manner are as
indispensable for some of the performers as power
and pathos are for others. The best of them only
walked through their parts on Saturday, a Miss
Floyd, who represented the lame girl in a quite
colourless fashion, being hoisted into the position
of a favourite simply because the house wanted to
applaud somebody. Clifton Alderson, the dook
and male “star” of the crowd, is a large,
showy, self-satisfied gentleman, who talks
like Knight Aston and resembles Charles
Warner in face and height. Miss Yilliers herself
would seem to be a lady possessed of sufficient
means to enable her to run her own show and
figure in large capitals on the playbill. It is in-
conceivable that she can have been seriously
engaged to come out here and make a fortune for
the new Royal proprietary. She lacks four essen-
tials for the name part in Jones’play. Although
evidently an experienced stage hand, she shows no
evidence of real ability as an actress. She is cer-
tainly not beautiful, she doesn’t look like a girl,
and her attempt at dancing is pathetic. She
starts to show the dook a new and beautiful leg-
shake •when they are on the island, and after the
fourth or fifth kick the virtuous Quaker admirer,
who spots his Drusilla just as ho is coming out of
a neighbouring prayer-meeting, shouts out,
“Woman, what art thou?” He might well ask
the question. Two or three others of the company
are harmless, and the balance ought to be taken
away in the dust-cart. To be continued in our
next.

Over at the Bijou ther-e was another
crowded meeting, on Saturday, in honour of
“ Dandy Dick,” and that trusty animal carried his
singed tail to victory as of yore. Sass made his
first appearance at the house in the capacity of
Major Tarver, a dyspeptic warrior who is pre-
pared to pack up a few charcoal biscuits and
toddle into the deadly bi-eech when called upon.
Also, Miss Brenda Gibson took up the pretty little
character of Sheba in a pleasing way, whilst the
heat and burden of the night was borne by the
originals. Mrs. Brough again ran riot around the
Deanery, and once more did Noah Brough, P.C.,
drag the Very Reverend Anson from under the
horse’s hoofs when he was about to give Dandy
half a pound of arsenic to cure his “ chill.” The
Brough and Boucicault trade-mark—three B’s in
a diamond—is stamped all over this performance
of Pinero’s. comedy, but its present reign
tei’minates to-night (30th). On Friday they put
up “Peril” for the first time in Australia.
“Peril” is the Bancroftian version of “Nos
Intimes,” differing from “Friends” (late of
Sydney) in sundry particulars.

“ The County Fair” pegs away feebly at
the Opera House, and there is little health in it.
People certainly seem glad to find that Sally has
lent Taggs another night-gown, because the
damsel would catch cold if she were to come
prowling downstairs in the ten inch garment she
wore before the play started. But they refuse to
take the great mechanical race at all seriously. It
makes a row like a watermill in action, and alto-
gether fails to look like an idyll of the turf.
Meanwhile, the Alexandra is monopolised by
painters and decorators. Dampier received a
cordial send-off from his patrons and a silver cigar-
case from the company and employes on Saturday.
Just now he is showing a varied assortment of
attractions round the Bendigo, Ballarat, and
Geelong districts. The Italian Opera season starts
on August Bth, and subscribers may book their
names until the day preceding the event.

Business has slowed down at Melbourne
Princess’, although the reduction in crush is not
yet sufficiently noticeable to have caused an
announcement of the second Gaiety burlesque—
“ Cinder Ellen,” A batch of “new features”
gave a flavour of novelty to “Huy Bias” on
Saturday. Of the old features, pretty Miss Levey
seems to be improving on acquaintance. Her
dancing is as light as a snowflake, and her arm
and wrist-play especially graceful. As a speaker
of lines, too, she is far more satisfactory than the
other airy fairy, Sylvia Grey, who .doesn't ,seem
to try to act as nicely as Fred. Leslie can whistle.
One or two very aged and infirm jokes have
dropped out of “Ruy Bias,” apparently. The
cold weather has mercifully parried them off,
maybe. On the other hand, a new jest fairly
shook the house on G.N. Hurdle race-night, when
Dauby observed that they must expect Crusoe to
win on a.Friday. The same stroke of wit had
flashed across Flernington a hundred times during
the afternoon, yet not one broken-up punter had
thought it the least bit brilliant. Nothing is
brilliant to the miserable sinner whose fancy has
just come to grief at the first jump, or failed to
“ stay ” home.
*» * *
At the Athemeum big-hearted John
Roberts gave a magnificent performance last
Saturday afternoon and evening, when he con-
ceded 1000 in 2000 to the local billiard champion,
Harry Evans, and finished well in front. The
masterly manner in which the “ star ” took all the
shine out of a player who seems a great artist in
any other company was a treat to witness. The
Flood Fund benefited by Roberts’ first week at
the Athenaeum to the extent of £215. He had
volunteered to contribute 25 per cent, of the
takings, like a man and a brother.

The cableman, who never forgets to
advise us when Lord Tompkins catches a cold in
the nose, might have sent word to Australia that
old friend Joe Emmett died on June 16th.
Pneumonia —aided and abetted by whisky, no
doubt—carried off this clever dancer, singer,
musician, female impersonator, and finished actor,
whose talent was strong enough to carry the
silliest of pieces half round the world, and wring
money out of it for well-nigh twenty years. A
comparatively sensible play like “ Hans the
Boatman,” would have suited Emmett down to
the ground, for he was master of the school in
which Arnold picked up his education. How-
ever, “Fritz” served, in its time, to send Aus-
tralia into convulsions over the gifted J. K., and
“Schneider how you vas ” lingers in many a
middle-aged heart to this day. Fritz’s song and
dance in prison, when he put the jailer’s kid on
his shoulder, was as pathetic as Maggie Moore's
mouth-organ performance to the incarcerated
Stoefel. He could change smiles into tears with
any artist that ever played Dutch Ameri-
can parts. At the London Adelphi, then
in very low water, Emmett made more fame for
himself than money for the show, but around the
provinces he must have done great business. The
prejudice against men-females (out of pantomime)
couldn't withstand Emmett’s realistic maiden who
used to tell an apocryphal Jacob to get along and
not bother her. With a fine pair of eyes, small
feet and graceful ankles, poor old Joe looked a lot
nicer than most of the girls one sees mashing
chappies, nor were his wig and complexion a bit
more artificial. But “Fritz” was a miserable
bundle of stuff to go travelling with, all the same.
A sort of music-hall drama, haunted by a wicked
colonel in an ulster, which reminds us that ’Gustus
Glover, now of Bland Holt’s company, imper-
sonated the aforesaid evil spirit in England. It
was awesome to hear him chuck his villainy off
h’s chest, and fearful to see him roll his gloomy
eyes at the genius who so recently embarked upon
the Jasper.
Our old friend, Henry Edwards, who recently died at
New York, had been staying at the Catskills for his
health, but the physicians there, seeing that the end was
near, advised him to return to his home. A few hours
after his arrival there, while suffering great pain, he said
to his devoted wife, “ Put your arms about me, dear,”
and suddenly expired. The immediate cause of death
was dropsy of the heart. He was born in 1824 at Ross,
Herefordshire, was the son of an attorney, and was at
first intended for the law, but drifted ihto amateur
theatricals (in company with Henry Irving, then Broad-
ribb), and finally on to the professional stage. It is need-
less to say anything about his Australian career in asso-
ciation with Brooke and Walter Montgomery. In 1878 he
supported Mary Anderson at the Boston theatre, playing
Master Walter to her Julia. He played with Mrs. Potter
in “ Antony and Cleopatra,” and afterwards visited
Australia as the Earl in “ Little Lord Fauntleroy.” He
was a famous entomologist, for some time edited the
leading entomological journal in the States, and enjoyed
the friendship of Agassiz. He won reputation as an
orator and author, and delivered funeral addresses over
the remains of John McCullough, Mary Fiske and others.
He left no relatives, and no family except his wife. He
was an agnostic, and his body w as cremated, according to
his wish. Joseph Jefferson, Harrison Fiske and George
Parsons Lathrop were among the pall-bearers, and many
distinguished literary people were around the grave. The
funeral oration was delivered by'William Winter.

THREE TRIOLETS.
I.— BEFORE THE PLAY.
(Now, one at a time, if you please !)
What we have to go through to see Sara!
This way to the stalls. What a squeeze !
(Now, one at a time, if you please !)
How the gods enjoy onions and cheese 1
Their taste in Olympus was rarer.
(Now, one at a time, if you please !)
What we have to go through to see Sara!
II.— THE INTERVAL.
(Lemou-squash threepence a glass !)
I wonder how long they will be?
They say she’s a widow at grass
(Lemon-squash threepence a glass !).
If we miss the last steamer, alas !
There will be no more outings for me.
(Lemon-squash threepence a glass !)
I wonder how long they will be?
III.— AFTER.
(Carriage ? What name, sir ? No, cab 1)
And the number, I fancy, is thirty.
Those French have the gift of the gab.
(Carriage? What name, sir? No, cab 1)
Was it poison she took or a stab?
Well, /couldn’t see anything dirty.
(Carriage? What name, sir ? No, cab 1)
And the number, I fancy, is thirty.
