“J.A.T.” (Junee): We are quite sure that we never
said in The Bulletin that we only favoured Woman
Suffrage because of the access of voting-power to the
National cause. On the contrary, we are and have
been, ever since we could think, keenly alive to the fact
that women’s votes would at first be largely controlled by
priestly and other castes. However, we recognise that
women must ultimately have votes, and the best way to
teach them how to use votes is to give them votes to play
with, just as the old man gives his young son a watch,
and trusts to Providence to keep the mainspring whole.
The world has to take its gruel “M. Dies”: Your
true cotton-wool bourgeois carefully eschews politics,
a.nd all public matters, devotes himself to making money,
lives in his own little Goshen, and gets more happiness out
of the world than anybody, by cunning selfishness..

require “R.T.” (Kelso): There is no discrepancy
between the details of coal-mine working in the “ Libera-
tion of the Three,” and in the Convict Narrative now
being published in a Sydney paper. The time of “ The
Liberation of the Three,” P. W.’s story, was laid in the
Commandancy of Dr. “ Rossell ” and the Governorship of
Colonel Arthur. The ex-convict speaks of a period seven
or eight years later, when coa! mines had been systemati-
cally opened out by Captain O’Hara Booth and were being
worked on Cornish lines. Lieutenant-Colonel Logan, of
the 63rd Regiment, ordered the shafts first to be sunk
without due regard to the scientific conditions, and the
methods of working were no more intelligent. The
original gangs of workers were selected according to their
class of crime.

The Victorian Assembly has dropped the
title “ Commonwealth ” from the Federation Bill
by 46 votes against 22, Graves, M.P., remarking
that “ Commonwealth ” meant Fenianism. It is
explained that in vetoing “ Commonwealth,” the
Assembly was “ only cursing Cromwell.”
John Roberts was on the whole only able
to give them an occasional suggestion of his real
powers during his series of exhibition games at
Melbourne Athenaeum. A long sea-voyage had
put the champion (who is getting very grey) con-
siderably out of form, yet he contrived to do some
beautiful work every now and then, especially
when he had a good house to play to. In this
direction the Hopetouns gave their gracious
assistance. Lady Hopetoun not only came on a
State visit with her liege lord to study nursery
cannons, but Tuesday of this week she was tc
come again on her own account. Roberts has
ever been tire billiard pet of the high and mighty,
for the reason that he conducts himself like a
gentleman and can always play up on special
occasions—the very thing that most of his
rivals cannot do.


""This narrative is mainly founded upon conversations
with a prominent official- he died only the other day—who
was for some years immediately associated with Colone
Arthur. Documentary evidence is, in addition, extant for
nearly every statement made in the course of the Tale,
and the words put into the mouth of the characters are
mere paraphrases of utterances of their originals. The
“police reports” and convict “records” quoted are
authentic in every particular; “ Longden’s ” letter
is that of a veritable convict. In the wording of the
Executioner’s Oath, the author has followed his informant
instead of transcribing another form which he has in his
possession, which, however, does not differ very materially
from the former. “The Sheriff’s bidding,” which con-
cludes the administration of the oath, was uttered, it is
asserted, on as recent an occasion in England as the
appointment of that late honored servant of the State,
Calcraft.

Tales of the Old Regime.
No. XII —A Day with Governor Arthur.*
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
Colonel George Arthur is sitting in an
arm-chair in his study. The deep whirr of a
dock (by Dent, London: you can see it, if not
hear it, to-day in a Hobart public office) as it
a trikes the hour of 10 is audible. He is always
punctual is Governor Arthur, Lieutenant-
Governor of Vandemonia always has been
always, will be. There are some men whose past
unerringly foretells their future. Arthur is oim of
these men. When, as representative of British
Majesty, he flogged a woman in his West Indian
principality, he concluded his order for the punish-
ment with the word “punctually.” When he
signs a death-warrant now, he hands it to the
Clerk of Council with the remark: “Let the
warrant be obeyed punctually.” When his
doctor tells him in the course of a few years that
Death will wait upon him after the expiration or
an hour, you may depend upon it, Sir George, as
he will be then, will simply request the medical
man to present his compliments to the gentleman
with the scythe, and desire him to be punctual.
And as he is in precept, so he is in practice.
At the first stroke of ten, then, His Excellency
sits him down, and begins the day’s routine. It is
an “off day,” that is to say, His Excellency d«pes
not this morning propose to do his Executive
Councillors the pleasure of going through the
form of conferring with them upon points as to
which he has already made up his mind. Conse-
quently, there being no Executive Council to.
bother him, he proceeds to act as the Executive. _
We are going to spend the day with him. It is
very rude of us, no doubt, as we have not been
invited. And particularly rude inasmuch as we
know that on the rare occasions that the Governor
departs from his repose of manner it is to consign
all scribblers to the nethermost abode of lost souls.
Frank Goddard, convict, otherwise “Frank tho
Poet,” peopled Hell with penal officials. Governor
Arthur peopled his hell with journalists.
• * *
It is a spare-framed, thin-faced, medium-
sized man who sits in the Governor’s chair ; clean-
shaven, save for a tinge of greyish whisker ; with
steely-grey eyes that look with a critical disbelief
in everything except the System; with a rasp in
the voice. The lines at the corners of the drawn-
down lips spell “Martinet”; the wrinkles of the
brow and the crevices between the eyebrows tell
that he is a precisian. At this present moment
he is supreme over the destinies of 32,000 human
beings, and he looks capable of dealing justly
with 32,000 blocks of pine or gum-wood. That,
possibly, is why he is Lieutenant-Governor of
His Britannic Majesty’s colony and dependency
of Van Dieman’s Land. A man with a heart
would have been out of place in that chair,
* * *
He touches a bell—twice. Mr. J. Fubster,
Comptroller-General of Convicts, enters—bows
deferentially—and is motioned to a chair.
“Good morning, Mr. Comptroller !”
A soft “Good morning, your Excellency, is
breathed by Fubster. He is at liberty to begin
now with business —and to seat himself.
“This day’s business, your Excellency, is appli-
cations for Tickets for November last.”
“Proceed, Mr. Comptroller!”
Standing up and clearing his throat, the Comp-
troller began : u Memorial No. 6GG. Police
Number, 699, James Melrose, per Malabar,
Middlesex Sessions, 30th J une, 182-, life, Hobart
Town, sth August, 182-, 7 years, same date, 7
years ; total term of transportation, life and 14
years. Employed by Mrs. C. Wade, and recom-
mended for ticket by her. Transported for
Burglary. His gaol report: ‘ln Newgate before,
and his hulk report was ‘ orderly.’ He stated this
offence, breaking into a house. Colonial con-
victipns are as follows; —August 5, 182-, by
Supreme Court, stealing a pair of boots; plea,
guilty; sentence, seven years’ transportation to
Macquarie Harbour. Same date and court, steal-
ing soap; plea, guilty; sentence, seven years’
transportation to Macquarie Harbour. March 27,
1827, exchanging his hat with prisoner who was
about to abscond, 50 lashes. June 27, 182-,
destroying his shirt, 25 lashes. October, 28, 182--,
not accomplishing his weekly task of work, 18
lashes. February 14, 1832, ordered 50 lashes by
employer for disrespectful conduct and in-
solence to Miss Ann Wade, 50 lashes, and returned
to service. November 20, 1832, drunk ad-
monished.”
“Who signs?”
“Mr. Muster-Master Mason, your Excellency.
“ What do you recommend ? ”
“I cannot recommend, sir.”
“Quite right, Mr. Comptroller. There seems
a hiatus in his record between 182- and 1832.
There must be some mistake there. It is impos-
sible that a man of this desperate character should
have committed no offences for several years !”
“ That’s what I think, your Excellency.”
“Reprimand the record clerk ——”
“ The record clerk is a transport, sir.”
“ A transport}! And to make such a serious
•omission ! It must be intentional, Mr. Comp-
troller. Have him punished, sir—send him to the
chain-gang.”
“ May I suggest, sir, that I should merely dock
his allowances and severely caution him? He’s a
very useful writer, sir, on the whole.”
“Oh, in that case, Mr. Comptroller, punish him
as you suggest. And note, please, that this
ticket is refused.”
And so was Mr. James Melrose’s chance of ob-
taining temporary freedom put back for at least
six, and probably twelve months. On the hypo-
thesis, as likely as not entirely erroneous, that the
record clerk had made a serious mistake in trans-
scribing, from the registers, on No. 699’s report
parchment the debits against his name, that per-
verse son of the System was doomed to a continued
period of slavery. What to precisian Colonel
Arthur, and what to disciplinarian Fubster,
was it, that the transport might be rendered
•desperate by the non-success of his mighty effort
to win a “ ticket ” by good conduct ? To the Men
of the Regime it should have been easy enough to
have read between the lines of the record. A
man naturally of weak disposition, made worse
by unspeakable surroundings and petty tyrannies
—with impulses towards good even at Macquarie
Harbour (witness that exchange of hats)—then
coming under a master or mistress of rnore_ than
average kindness of heart whom he would, in the
intervals of good conduct, brave by an occasional
act of insolence or insubordination —that was this
character obviously to men who understood the
System.
Now, Colonel Arthur and Factotum Fubster
BREAKING THE RECORD.
MRS. McCANTY : “An’ ye’ve raised quoite a big family, Mrs. Murphy?”
MRS. MURPHY (with pride): “Sivin polacemin, Mrs. McCanty.”
professed to know the System. They knew, How-
ever, but the one side of it. The gap in No.
699’s record was to them nothing but a convict-
writer’s error. That it could be accounted foi
bv Melrose’s abstinence from ill-doing—pooh,
impossible ! And so No. 699 was “ put back.”
* * *
“Next!”
“Memorial No. 667. Cole Richard, Lancaster
Assizes, 22 April, 182-, life. Application recom-
mended bv Mr. J. England, J. A. Jackson, Esq.,
J. Simpson, Esq., and Mr. R. Court. Now
employed in Freed Police, Campbell Town. Tran-
sported for stealing from the person ; gaol-report,
‘twice convicted before’-hulk-report, ‘good.’
Stated this offence, pocket-picking; denies having
been convicted before. Colonial Convictions :
November 2, 182-, Supreme Court, aiding and
abetting in assault on a woman, not guilty.
January 11, 182-, Magistrate Fisher, neglect of
duty for the last 3 days, 50 lashes and 3rd class.
Feb 8, 182-, absent from his gang, between 10 and
11 o’clock this day, 25 lashes ; April 23, 182-,
disobedience of orders and other improper con-
duct, 25 lashes. Feb. 3, 182 -, W. Abel, magis-
trate, assault, 25 lashes. March 10, 182-, same
magistrate, threatening and abuse, 6 months
chain-gang and return to his master. June
18 182 -, Chain-gang overseer, for being in
state of intoxication at muster, sentenced
him to 21 davs’ additional chain - gang.
March 11, 182 , Abel, magistrate, neglect
of duty; one month of irons at New Norfolk.
March 31, 183 , in Public Works Gang, very
riotous conduct, and assaulting watchman ; tran-
sported to penal settlement for two years. June 16,
182-, by penitentiary superintendent for reporting
himself as sick, and not having anything the
matter with him; reprimanded. December 26,
183 , by superior officer of Eield Police, neglect of
duty ; fined 10s.”
“ Who signs? ”
“ Muster-Master Mason. ” )(
“ And your recommendation is ? ”
“ I regret, your Excellency, that this man has
been allowi dto join the police. The circum-
stances attending the assault on the woman for
which he was tried are still strong in my recollec-
tion, as it occurred in my own neighbourhood.”
“But he was found not guilty, did you not
say ? ”
“ Yes, sir, but ” ,
“Then, why say anything about the offence .
demanded the just Governor. He was always
just was Arthur ! A desire to be just was his
strong point, he used to say.
“ Apart from that altogether though, Mr.
Comptroller,” the Governor went on, as Fubster
remained silent, “ the applicant must be refused.
The man seems a most improper subject for the
police; but, being in it, the opportunity may be
afforded him to prove that he is a reformed
character.”
“Yes, your Excellency. Shall I minute that
observation ?”
“Certainly. Now the next.”
And, delaying just a minute to record the fact
that Richard Cole, No. 473, per Elphinstone,
would be allowed to derive further advantage
from the moral associations of the Freed Police,
the Comptroller-General passed to another of the
current applications for tickets.
“ Memorial No. 668, Police No. 1701, Henfrey
Simon, .tried at Nottingham, March, 182 , life.
Recommended by Messrs. Hill, Gunning, and
Butcher. Employed by Messrs. Signed and Hill.
Transported for sheep-stealing. Gaol report,
‘ not known before ; good in gaol. * Hulks report,
‘orderly.’ Stated his offence sheep-stealing.
Hath no colonial offence recorded. Mason signs.”
“No offence?” queried His Excellency. “ Shall
we give him a ticket?”
“ Well, sir, I think not. He has not served the
regulated period,” rejoined the Comptroller-
General.
“ Refused, then !” said the Governor. And No.
1761 was “ put back ” also.
“ Memorial No. 669. Police No., 2693.
Porter John. Tried at Stafford, 17 Oct., 182—.
Seven years. Recommended by J. T. Gelhbrand,
Esq., who employs him. Transported for stealing
from the person. Gaol Report: ‘An incorrigible
youth ; has been many times in prison, and con-
nected with Wolverhampton thieves. Orderly in
gaol.’ Stated his offence, ‘ Stealing five shillings
from a young woman; twice for leaving my
master.’ Hath no colonial offences on record.
The Muster-Master signs.”
“ What do you say, Mr. Comptroller ?”
“This, your Excellency, is another case, where
applicant has not served the regulated period.”
“The transport is incorrigible, eh? Still, we
must encourage him to hope, conditional on im-
provement. Refused at present, but as he brought
so bad a character, it is gratifying to perceive that
his conduct has been uniformly correct. Grant
him a ticket on the King’s birthday. Wo must
never shut out hope altogether from the convicts,
Mr. Comptroller. That has always been my
policy, as you know. And I am stating it at
great length to Lord Goderich by the outgoing
mail. Are there many more applications’”
“Some fifty, your Excellency. The next has
some peculiar circumstances connected with it,
sir. The applicant, of fair conduct enough,
obtained his master’s frank to the cover of the
application, which was directed, of course, to
the Muster-Master, and then pasted a slip
over the address. Upon the slip there was a
British address, and inside the form he enclosed
a letter for an English friend ! ”
“ The rascal! ” exclaimed his Excellency. “ And
how was the fraud detected ? ”
“Well, sir, the ignorant fellow thought his
master’s signature would frank the letter out of
the colony as well as within it! That not being
the case, the Post-office stopped the letter, sent it
back to Captain Hepburn, the transport’s master,
and, of course, the thing was discovered.”
“Then, how does the application come before
me? An application from such a man—why,’tis
scandalous ! ”
“ So I remarked, your Excellency, but Captain
Hepburn said he was prepared to overlook the
wrong because of the man’s ingenuity and the good
tone of his letter. I have the letter here. Shall
I read it, sir?”
His Excellency assented, and Mr, Eubster read
this ;—
St. Pauls Plains, Van Demans Land.
May 6th.
Dear George,—This is now the first time that I have
had the opportunity of writing to you since my arrival in
this Forighn Land" you desired me to write and Let you
Know how I am doing. I must first tell you of my voyage
across. There was Three Hundred of us poor fellows con-
fined in one Deck 6 in a bed with very little to eat or
Drink but I had better luck than the rest for I had a situa-
tion on board to serve out stores to the men so I Got on
very well we had a very Good voyage of four Months we
were all sent to a place called a tench and there we were
signed off to Defferent masters I am cook at Mr Hebron
Esq Dear George you cannot forme any Idea of the Treble
1 have been 1 had to wear Heavy Irons on my Legs for 3
months because I should not run away this is an aweful
country for you dont Know when you are safe for there
are men prowling about who stile themselves Bushrangers
who when they are Hungry come to our Huts and tye our
Hands and then proseed to rob and plunder the House
and if we offer any resistance they will Shoot you dead on
the spot they rob the House and then make one of us
carry the spoil into the bush. there w'as three at
large last week who have been doing Horrible deeds
among which they went into a publick House in
the interior of this County not far from where there.
Dear Friend I have that Bible still which your
beloved parrent gave me and every time I look at it my
heart Bleads to think how happy and comfortable 1 was
then. Remember me to your kind and affectionate Wife
& Family & Mrs Barton, and Tom tell him from me to he
steady and industrous and not to be led away by the
girls as they will shure to bring him ruin. Give my love
to all the family arm I hope and trust they will forgive
me for my ingratitude to them after the Kindness they
had shone to me Dear George would you he so Kind as
to go to Mr Greenwood for me and remember me Kindly
to Him & Family and tell him were I am as he promised
to do a little for me ask him to ask my uncle to send me
an accordian as I think It might be of serv ice to me here
as there is none in the county, I have seen. Remember
me to all my Friends that inquier after me Dear George
write to me as soon as you can as I long to here from you
how things are going on at home .1 hope you will pardon
me for Troubleing you with a letter But I Know you to
be a Friend that I can Trust When you write to me
Direct to Capt. Hepburn Esq. Rays Hill St Pauls Plains'
Van Demans Land
1 have nothing more to say at Present May God Prosper
you and your Family Adieu
D. G. be shure and wite A. S. Longden.
N. B. The master I am With is Capt. Hepburn, Esq. J.P.
Brother-in-Law to Thos. Hosie Esq. Manager at Millers
Wharf. London.
“Well!” exclaimed Arthur as Eubster con-
cluded the reading. “ And in face of that letter,
the man’s ma ter recommends him for a ticket!
Remove the employer from the list of eligibles,
and send the man to the chain-gang.”
“ Yes, your Excellency, but—ah, may I suggest,
sir, that the Captain has great—er —home in-
fluence ? ”
“H’m, ah ! Perhaps the case, then, as regards the
master will be met by a respectful letter pointing
out that discipline will be subverted if he displays
the like tolerance in the future. Why, he actually
compounds a felony—obtaining a postal frank by
false pretences is a felony, is it not ? ”
The Comptroller hesitates in his reply, and then
begs to suggest that as the law is not quite explicit
on the point, the question should be referred to
the Crown-Solicitor. “’Tis an unusual offence,
your Excellency—can scarcely have been antici-
pated by the Honorable the Council.”
“Nevertheless, Mr. Comptroller, once done the
deed will be repealed. Mr. Solicitor shall prepare
a draft-clause making the offence felony.”
“ And the man, sir—the transport Longden.”
“ I leave him to your jurisdiction, Comptroller.
But, of course, ticket refused, and, I should think,
the chain-gang.”
And at a later period of the day Transport
Longden is awarded, in his absence, twelve
months’ gang “for abusing his master’s con-
fidence.”
# * #
The ingenious Longden having been thus

“ Dead ? Oh, was that the yard-arm case .'
“Yes, your Excellency !”
“ Ah, I recollect now. And was nothing done
to the Surgeon?”
“Oh yes, sir. We were short of doctors at the
time, and so he was put on the permanent staff.
It was Dr. Burleigh, sir !”
“ Burleigh, was it? A good officer and a care-

past. But proceed, Mr. Comptroller.”
“She is recommended by Mr. J. Hutchinson
and A. Turnbull, Esquire. She says her gaol
report was bad, and that she was capitally con-
victed at Newgate, receiving sentence of death,
but commuted to two years in the penitentiary
and fourteen beyond seas. States offence as steal-
ing silver teapot. Colonial report: Theft, com-
mitted for trial.”
“You recommend?”
“Yes, your Excellency. The female is at pre-
sent in the House of Correction under sentence.
But the Surgeon wishes her to take the situation
of nurse at the hospital, as she is skilful and kind-
hearted.”
“Under what sentence, did you say?”
The Comptroller re-scans the parchment and
the application-form, but the information is not
given in either document.
“Unstated, sir, I’m sorry to say !”
“Unstated, Mr. Comptroller! Is this a way
to prepare a report for me ? Her record says
‘committed for trial.’ How was she then dis-
posed of ?” , . „
“She must have oeen sentenced, sir! ihe
Comptroller sees he is “in for it,” and not en-
joying the Colonel in one of his black moods,
mentally resolves to make somebody suffer. “And
the convict-writer must have omitted, to enter the
conviction,”
“See that he’s punished, Mr. Comptroller,
And reprimand the free clerk in charge of the
office !”
“Yes, your Excellency. We must make an
example ! So much depends on the records being
accurately kept. What are your orders as to this
woman, sir? The doctor wishes her services
particularly.”
“ No, in this case I must refuse ! Appointment
to a nursing post would be an indulgence the
woman does not merit. She herself says her
home record was bad, and most likely her trans-
portation-sentence was life, and that she is lying
when she says it was for fourteen years only. No
indulgence !”
Thus it was nearly always. A transport’s state-
ment against himself or herself was almost in-
variably accepted by the Authorities, but he or
she was never believed when there was a possi-
bility of the statement being in the transport’s
own favour.
Thus, too, was it generally in the matter of
negligently-written-up records. It was a convict-
writer who was punished ; it was the Free Clerk,
or the other official, who was really the offending
person, who got off scot free. Which was just;
for a convict-writer was by virtue of the fact
liable to punishment. The Regime did not
recognise convict-writers, and, indeed, distinctly
prohibited their employment; and, therefore, if
they consented to be employed they disobeyed
“ the Regulation in that case made and provided.”
The circumstance that they dare not refuse to be
employed when so directed by an authority—say.
the Governor or the Comptroller-General—went,
of course, for nothing. If there was one .thing
more than another the System was partial to, it
was the construction of dilemmas; and though
the transport might object to being impaled on
one horn, there was always the other at his con-
venience.
* * *
While we have been making this digression,
Colonel Arthur has been refusing more and more
applications, occasionally, it must be said, varying
the process by an'approval. One “ Approved ”to
10 “ Refused,” that was about the average.
The list ended, the Governor condescended to
consult the inferior (though still potent) official,
as to the terms of a despatch he was drafting to
Viscount Goderich, Secretary of State for the
Colonies.
“ I chiefly desire, Mr. Comptroller, your
opinion on this portion. It is with reference to
the special and educated convicts, and their pro-
posed treatment at Port Arthur. I am loth to
abandon Macquarie Harbour as a Penal Settle-
ment—we have derived great advantage from it,
but the filling up of the bar is rendering naviga-
tion dangerous. So I have made up my mind to
direct the abandonment of the Harbour and to
devote my attention to all the measures best
calculated to render Tasman’s Peninsula, which
really seems to have been designed by Providence
for a penitentiary, a perfect institution. Do you
follow me, Mr. Comptroller ?”
“ Oh, most readily, sir. And if I may be per-
mitted to say so, if anyone can devise a perfect
penal establishment I am sure 'tis your Ex-
cellency !”
“Yes, Mr. Comptroller, I do not doubt my
success, if I can depend upon the willing co-
operation of able and experienced officers like
myself!”
“ That, your Excellency, I need scarcely
trouble to assure you of. The Staff are only too
proud to have the opportunity of working under
so brilliant and—er—h’m—philanthropic an ad-
ministrator ! You were saying about the specials,
sir?”
“ Oh, yes,” and the pleased Governor, who, with
all his strength of character, was never to be
offended by the most fulsome ot compliments,
especially when they alluded to his philanthropy,
went on to read what he had written.
44 This is what I say to his Lordship in recom-
mending the despatch to Port Arthur of this
special class. 4 The educated class are at present
the link by which the free and the bond are so
amalgamated as to make the distinction betwixt
slavery and freedom less marked than it ought to
be. It is desirable there should be no such
mingling : a man should be either free or bond,
and be treated as such.’ ”
44 Very good, if I may say so, your Excellency.
Most admirably put.”
The Governor continued : 44 4 1 have long disap-
proved of the employment of convict clerks under
any circumstances, and have taken every prudent
means of limiting their numbers, for I am con-
vinced that convict discipline cannot be con-
sidered perfect until every special is treated as an
ordinary convict.’ ”
44 That’s true, your Excellency.”
4 4 4 For if one receive indulgence irregularly, or
be placed in any easy situation, enabling him to
assume a respectable appearance, the System is
broken in upon, and a single representation to his
friends in England will be sufficient to deprive
transportation of much of its terrors. Criminals
do not, when writing to their friends, dwell upon
the hardships they suffer, but rather delight in
holding up the bright side of the picture.’ ”
44 Quite accurate, your Excellency, quite !” Yet
Longden’s letter was before them !
4 4 4 As it appears specials dread transportation
less than any other class, I have made arrange-
ments for their deportation upon arrival hereto
Tasman’s Peninsula, should such be the pleasure
of his Majesty’s Government, where they will be
deprived even of the hope of eluding, by artifice,
the miseries they have drawn upon themselves.’
There, how will that do, Mr. Comptroller ? ”
44 It must carry conviction to the Imperial
Government, your Excellency, of the wisdom of
your proposed sten.”
44 Yes, I think it will. And the Right Honor-
able the Secretary of State will not fail to appre-
ciate an argument I propose to communicate
unofficially, that the presence in the streets of
this city of special convicts, who, tor
the most part, come from the upper
order of society, is a direct inducement
to the circulation of those revolu-
tionary ideas which are so subversive
of Law, Order and Society. How
can the lower classes retain respect
for the upper orders, if members of
the latter are seen walking public
streets under the convict ban ? ”
“Very true, your Excellency, very
true!”
“Then you agree, Mr. Comptroller,
generally with my views.”
“Oh, yes! your Excellency, I can
suggest no improvement.”
“Then send the clerk who is your
best penman to me in the morning. I t
is a most important despatch as it
quite alters our System, and I must
have a clear copy for Downing-street
made with the utmost care.”
“ Shall the Clerk be ‘ free ’ or 4 bond,’
sir ? ”
44 Oh, your best.”
“Then, the ablest man I have, your
Excellency, is Convict-writer Dallas."’
44 Well, let him be here at ten punc-
tually to-morrow. And now,” rising
as he spoke, 41 this completes your
business with me for the day ? ”
44 Yes, sir, with the exception of
one little matter the executioner’s
oath.”
44 Ah ! I was forgetting ! Is the man
in attendance? If so, produce him.”
# * *
Convict Richard Johnson, suc-
cessor to Dougherty in the onerous
and well-paid post of hangman to His
Majesty’s Government in Vande-
monia, had shown a tendency of late
to partiality in the performance in his
duties. After the last but two 44 drop-
ping off,” it was rumoured that one of
the criminals who had, it was sup-
posed, been hurried into the next
world, had been resuscitated within
the time prescribed for the suspen-
sion of the body. The rumour as
to this occurrence had not reached
the ears of the Authorities for three
or four weeks after the ceremony, and
owing to the lapse of that time and to
the admixture of quicklime with the
bodies which participated in the rites,
it had been deemed inexpedient to
attempt exhumation. The Authorities,
though content to let the matter—and
the bodies—rest, thought it, however,
advisable to prevent a further instance of the like
partiality, and concluded that there was no better
way of securing their end than by imposing what
had for some years dropped into disuse—the
executioner’s oath.
Mr. Johnson, being so important a functionary
and the occasion being so novel, it was thought well
to impress him by administering the oath in the
overawing presence of the Governor himself. Ihe
formula was to be the ancient English one, and
the incidents of the scene were framed after the
same pattern. Only, instead of the Testament
being tendered to the Executioner by the Sheriff,
as was the old English practice, the Comptroller-
General, being the convict’s nominal master and
the man to whom Johnson was in the last resort
responsible, was to administer the oath.
Johnson was ushered in. He brought with him,
pursuant to instructions, a bag.
He saluted, and in obedience to a motion of
Mr. Fubster’s hand, arranged the contents
of the bag on a table. Two pairs of hand-
cuffs, a set of double leg-irons with bazils and
centre-link complete, and a new fifteen-foot rope,
gathered in a coil. A white cap, and a black cap
completed the number of the dread insignia.
“ You arc Richard Johnson, per ship Mangles?
demanded the Comptroller.
The executioner pulled his forelock.
“You are the public Executioner of His
Majesty’s Colony and Dependency of Van
Dieman’s Land?”
Johnson scraped his right foot, and bent his
head.
“ Hitherto you have been unsworn, but it has
been thought'necessary by his Excellency the
Governor that you should take an oath for the
faithful, exact, true, and impartial performance of
your duties. Are you willing to take that obliga-
tion ?”
“Yes, y’r Honor !”
The Comptroller-General lifted a Testament—
there were always copies available in every public
office ; oaths were taken at every hour of the day-
and, opening the Book at one of the Gospels, bade
Johnson place his left hand on the rope, and take
the Book in his right.
“ Now, Johnson, follow me !” As the Comp-
troller read these words from a slip of blue paper,
Johnson recited them after him.
44 1, Ri hard Johnson, Transport, acting under
a ticket - of-leave as present Executioner, do
willingly undertake to well, faithfully, exactly
and truly perform the duties appertaining to the
office of the Common Executioner of His Majesty s
Colony of Van Dieman’s Land, and do now vow
and make oath that I will carry out and fulfil such
duties as the Law may direct upon the bodies and
persons of every felon committed to my charge
without fear or favour, though the body and
person be that of friend or kin, my father or my
mother, my sister or my brother, my wife or my
child, or other bone of my bone, or of other flesh
of my flesh, So help me God.”
Then he kissed the Book.
The Comptroller turned to His Excellency.
44 Must the—ah—old command be given, your
Excellency ? ” . , „ ,
The Governor motioning assent, the Comptroller,
raising his right-hand, and speaking with as much
harshness as he could throw into his voice, cried :
44 Get Thee Hence, Wketch!”

English courts sit 213 days per annum.

£24,000; 1 Master of the Rolls, £6OOO ; 5 Lords
Justices (£5000), £25,000; 5 Chancery Judges

total salary-list of £178,000.
THE FIRST REAL VICTORIAN MAJOR.
It was Catherine de Medici who first
insisted upon smooth stockings. Up to her time
they were worn as are gloves to-day, very much
wrinkled. Evidently the new fashion was liked,
for to have a wrinkled stocking has been a disgrace
to womankind ever since.
In the autobiography of that philanthropic
old physician, Dr. Singleton (Melb.), it is related
how the wife of the executed convict Melnotte,
a Frenchwoman, named Alphonsine, got the body
and put it in her Little Bourke-street window
decorated with flowers, all day, and at night sur-
rounded it with tapers. A grand wake was held ;
after this episode no more bodies of the executed
were handed over to relatives.
The South Australian papers report the
painful case of a gentleman who got lost in the
bush near Silverton. It was pitch-dark, so he lie
a fire and camped for the night—only unfort
tunately he took an epileptic fit and fell into th-
blaze, and lay there combusting. When he woke
up his right hand was nearly burnt off, and the
arm shrivelled up. Then, being completely
bushed, he had to sit by the fire and shriek in a
mournful key for the rest of the night, and the
emu heard him howling, and the wild dog yelled
in reply, and the lonesome wilds were tilled with
notes of woe. Next morning he rode about
twenty-five miles to get the stump amptuated,
and after that he laid up in an hospital. The
hanging-reporters of the evening papers are
invited to consider this incident, and fill in details
about the strange shadows and dreary echoes of
that midnight encampment, and the gruesome
dreams of the frizzled traveller as he sat in his
horrid solitude, and the odour of burning flesh,
and the hollow, far-off sighing of the breeze upon
the mountains’ and the murmurs of the stream,
and all the rest of the properties.

The late Peter Carmichael, of Victoria,
was once inspecting a ragged infant-class. A
little beggar, who was being put through the
alphabet, stuck fast at the letter Q. “Come,
come,” said Carmichael, helpingly, “ You know
that feller right enough; just think a while;
don’t hurry.” “Know him? Course I does. X
know the (maggory daggery) thing right enough,
but I can’t remember his (waggery snaggery
baggery) monniker!”
This yarn is being told of the late Thos.
Cox Bray, father of Chief Secretary Bray, of
S.A, At a meeting of the Burra Mine direc-
torate, years ago, Chairman Solomon savagely
blurted out: “ Look here, Bray, for £5 I’d knock
that blessed head of your’s straight.” “ Done! ’’
cried Bray, as he put down a fiver, and insisted
on the mill, which did not, however, cure him of a
ridiculous habit for which he was noted of carry-
ing his head very much on one side like a grinning
fox-terrier.
Suppose yourself the happy possessor of
two millions of money. What would you do
with it ? Would you follow Mr. Paling’s noble
deed and build a sanatorium for the sick ? or
would you follow the lead of “ Jubilee ” Benson
and play it up, and finish in a continental
gaol ? Fancy what you could do with two
millions. You could visit and view the sights of
the old and new worlds, you could keep a
splendid establishment, have scores of servants
at your beck and call, you could keep your
horses and your hounds, your guns, your yachts,
your town and country houses, you could hunt,
fish and travel and live a life of continual
pleasure if—Oh ! that “if” it always creeps in
—if you had good health ! If you had bad
health and could not enjoy your great posses-
sions, what good would all the money and
riches in the terrestrial world be worth to you ?
good health is the great desideratum— preserve
it while you have it, for it may be lost for ever
if you neglect it. While on this subject we
want a few serious words with you. Every
man and woman is the centre of the world to
himself, therefore look after yourself and those
belonging to you, preserve your strength and
take note of the small particulars of daily life.
We want to impress this particularly upon you
—you, reader—we are addressing you personally
—take care of the small signs of constitutional
disturbance, don’t neglect a simple headache
because it is so general, such pains cannot be in
a healthy organism, physical disturbance can-
not be where all organs are performing their
functions with clock-work exactitude. Like a
small spark spreading into a great conflagration,
disease begins in a little way, so little that its
first onslaught is frequently unnoticed, but it
generally extends, until too lath. Take good
advice, attack the first signs by a vigorous
treatment with Clements Tonic, which so
renovates and fertilises the blood, that it will
quickly restore the impaired power of any
organ or tissue. Clements Tonic is a scientific
combination. Its therapeutic effects are proved.
That it will allay and prevent disease is beyond
all doubt, the praise bestowed on it over the
signatures of the highest and most influential
people in the land, will convince the most
sceptical. Mr. F. Bagnell, Ravenhurst, Mar-
rickville, N.S.W., write us: “Dear Sir, —
Allow me to add my testimony to the many
endorsements you have already received, proving
the excellence of Clements Tonic. My wife
has been suffering for the past two years from
weakness and debility, and has tried many
treatments without result, she, as a last resort
commenced the use of Clements Tonic, and I
am thankful to say the effect has been most
gratifying, and I am firmly persuaded that it is
a complete restoration of perfect health.” A.
G. Taylor, Esq., M.L.A., writes; —“Parlia-
ment House, Sydney, Nov. 13th, ’90.—1 have
much pleasure in testifying to the benefits I

Having been afflicted with extreme nervous

Society
As The Bulletin enthusiastically pre-
dicted would be the case, the Privy Council upset
(on the contention raised by this paper, and not
on the legal objection raised at the hearing of the
appeal at Sydney) the decision of the N.o.
Fall Court in the case of Dr. M‘Leod, found
guilty of having committed bigamy in America—
thus upholding the opinion of Judge Windeyer
against that of Chief Justice Darley and Judge
limes. The case was, from the very first, as clear
as daylight to every lawyer and level-headed lay-
man. That a man should have been imprisoned
for 14 months, pending the decision of such a
transparent matter, is an indelible disgrace to the
N.S.W. Bench. The dunder-headedness shown
in the M‘Leod case by the majority of the Court
would have discredited the weakest-headed mem-
ber of the Articled Clerics’ Association.
* * «
M‘Leod has now been released. The
Privy Council decision says he is to get costs.
From whom, is a mystery.
# * *
Died of a rush of his own high collar to
the head! At a Southern coffee palace last
week a man was found in bed in a strangled
condition, He had on all his clothes, including a
portentous collar that went up about his ears, and
the conclusion arrived at was that he had rashly
lain down and gone to sleep in full costume, and
the collar had asphyxiated him. The deceased was
aged only 35 years, and was thus cut off in the
flower and blossom of his existence, treacherously
slain by his own linen in the dark. His togs came
silently upon him in that hour when night is
calmest, and laid him low. There was nothing
odorious about his end ; if he had died on a battle-
field, or had stood on the deck of a sinking ship,
or perished amid the ruins of a falling kingdom
with burning palaces collapsing on his head, he
might have made a passing sensation, and would
probably have left some footprints on the sands of
Time, but he -was only shot by his shirt in an
attic, and dropped into an obscure grave. His
fate is a warning to the rising generation to
beware of the tall collar that walketh in darkness
and the immense starched bosom that lays for its
victim in shadowy places.
# * *
If Bellew has really married Mrs. Brown-
Potter, the deed was doubtless done in order that
the lady might not want for a name to travel
upon. James Brown-P. objected, we imagine, to
being advertised all around the globe by a fair
divorcee, more especially as he may have a mind
to start another real Mrs. Potter of his own at an
early date. Wherefore Curly, for chivalrous
and professional reasons, also because he couldn’t
help it, followed his leading lady into a registrar's
office and made her Mrs. B’lew.
Sydney society will be shocked before long
by a tale of perfidy with the inevitable woman in
it. Strangely enough the usual man isn’t in it at
all on this occasion.

A lively scene took place the other day in
a Sydney grill-room. A Mr. Blank was seated at
a table diligently shovelling down his capacious
maw a shilling’s-worth of “chops and taters,”
oblivious of the exits and entrances of the motley
crowd that elbowed its way in and out, Entered
a Mr. Billabong (who knew Blank casually), just
well enough “corned ”to be nasty. Now, one of
Blank’s peculiarities-is to use his knife when,
according to the “strict rules,” he should em-
ploy his fork. Billabong, observing this, gently
pointed it out to him ; Blank heeded not, but
muttered something about people minding their
own business, and, regardless of the feelings of
. . , ./ s
Billabong, continued to shovel away with hi
blade. This was too much for the latter (who
used to be a great “ entertainer ”in the good old
days). “For God’s sake, Blank, stop it; you’ll
cut your blanky head off!” he ejaculated. No
response from old Blank, who still “ kept at it.”
Words proving useless, Billabong seized a loaf of
bread from the table, and, taking aim, made a
cannon off Blank’s bald pate on to a big glass
window. Then Billabong and Blank “went for ”
each other, and the game didn’t finish till the
police were called in. Blank does not stoke him-
self there now.
* • #
Whafc a dread rich Australians have of the
British income-tax commissioners! This accounts,
no doubt, for the fact that so many gilded
“colonists” prefer to “pig it” in hotels rather
than take comfortable private houses (as they did
in the country where they made their cash) and
risk a visit from the tax-collector. For instance,
J F ——, an Australian millionaire, lives at
one of Bailey’s hotels in London, and does not
even indulge in the luxury of taking his meals in
comfort in a private sitting-room. He is one of
the most solidly wealthy of the absentee brigade,
and yet is ever haunted by a morbid fear that he
will die a beggar.

It will interest his friends to learn that a
certain rich Australian brewer who is starring it
in England, now eats “pays” with his fork.
Those new-fangled “ sparrowgrass ” tongs are,
however, he says, “ the very divil.”

A Melbourne firm of “ universal pro-
viders ” were wickedly hoaxed, the other day,
bv a bogus letter from one of Hopetoun’s aides
requesting two tons of meat, more or less, to be
carted up to Govingment ’Ouse as soon as possible.
The entire butchery staff of the establishment was
ordered to set about trussing the requisite number
of fatted calves and things, and when the new
extra special waggon had been carefully loaded up
with carcases, and the horses ornamented with
choice rosettes, the boss partner called for his
patent-leather boots, and proceeded to tool the
caravan along himself. On arriving at Hopetoun’s
fortress, however, the jubilant dealer in animal
food was received in a cold, haughty manner by
the commander-in-chief of the cooks, who stated
that the garrison was already full victualled and
wanted no more meat. Also the noble young aide
staggered forth, with the firm's letter of thanks in
his hand, and suggested that there must be some
confounded mistake somewhere, haw ! Where-
upon the deluded non-purveyor of flesh to his
Excellency drove home again, accompanied by his
body-guard of butchers, and the two tons of meat
were cast loose upon the world at an alarming
sacrifice whilst the rosettes were recklessly given
away with pounds of butter.
Tho N.S.W. Registrar-General, finding
such a state of things inconsistent with the
dignity of a Government establishment, has
ukased that in future none of his myrmidons shall
work in their shirt-sleeves.

A titled female some time ago went into a
Brisbane store run on democratic principles, and
started the old game of having half the stun
hauled over the counter for her inspection. Ihe
shopman stood it for a time, but at last he said,
“Come, hurry up, ma’am, please. I cant stop
with you all day.” She replied, 44 You evidently
don’t know me. I’m Lady ——I don t care
who you are. We can’t waste time on people who
don’t seem to know what they want,” rejoined
the unassuming purveyor of soft-goods. She left
with an aching void in her pure merino chest.

sington, London, on Hospital-Sunday, was the
largest on record. No doubt this is owing to the
number of Australians who attend that church.
Saul Samuel carried one of the collection-bags.
But how does he get into that gallery ?
* * *
A man was “ lumbered ” the other day, in
northern N.S.W. for stealing a feather mattrass.
Collecting mattrasses seemed to be a mania with
him, for some twenty or thirty were found in his
hut. He intimated that he was in search of a
wife. Two months. Probably mad as a hatter.
# # *
A few years ago an Adelaide parson and
temperance-lecturer made a fortune by invest-
ments in mining stock. Not satisfied with his
pile, he started as a broker, and also invested a
large sum in a brewery, throwing up his church.
Now he is a brewery-clerk at £2 a week.

At latest dates there were rowdy crowds
ever ready to receive Tummy outside London
railway-stations, feeding halls, picture galleries,
or other public places where his royal nibs was
expected. The first, glimpse of his large fleshy
countenance would be hailed with shouts of
“’Ave yer brought the counters with yer?” or
“’Ow did yer git on at baccyrar last night?”
Even more trying for the Coming K. was the
inevitable wag who would address loud remarks
to an imaginary friend, such as “ Bill, the counters
is inside ’is bell-topper,” or “Do yer twig em
bulging out his coat-pocket, Charley ?’ ihe lory
dread of the Tranby Croft scandal coming into
Court, and so being brought right under the notice
of the “people” was evidently well-founded.
While the vague story of Tummy’s little
weakness was confined to sassiety papers, which
circulate only amongst the uppah and loyal
bourgeois classes, it didn’t matter much. But,
when full and particular details of the big
case came to be hawked around the kingdom for
a penny, or the still humbler “brown, the tat
fell into the fire of democracy, causing derisive
tongues of flame to dart forth in all directions.
German William’s recent visit must have been a
godsend for his bald-headed uncle. It enabled
the latter to show in public, under cover of that
“splendid reception ” which the British shop-
keepers always administer to any foreign
potentate whose presence is, according to fusty
fiction, a “ good thing for trade.”

Mrs. P. and Kyrle Bellew,
Is this pretty story true?
Have you joined hearts and hands
Far away in furrin lands?
Are you, tel! us, madam, pray,
Sworn to honour and obey
Kyrley of the grizzled locks.
Gushing voice, and silken socks ?
Tell us truly, Mister K.,
Have you pitched yourself away—
Have you gone and said “ I will ” ?
Better to be single still.
Marriage is a step insane
When there’s nothing left to gain ;
Why, then, make, when once you’ve got her,
Mrs. B’lew of Mrs. Potter?
Fellows say there’s fun in life
Hugging someone else’s wife ;
But they say the pleasure's flown
If you have to hug your own.
Cupid lingers, artful thing!
Near you till you buy the ring;
Ere that ring begins to wear
Cupid’s gone—he isn’t there.
THE GREAT INEVITABLE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Young Kitty was a dashing maid,
So witty, bright, and “snappy,”
Enough of ankle she displayed
To make the strollers happy.
I used to like to take her out,
We sometimes had a carriage ;
But, though she tried to hook her trout,
I wasn’t “ on ” for marriage.
I often dined with her mama
(A pleasant home the gal had),
Then Kit would bring a good cigar,
And sing a touching ballad.
One couldn’t help but like her way,
For chaps are only human ;
Till ten, or twelve, I’d often stay
And ogle that young woman.
She’d pinch my cheek and call me Kirl,
I shrank from pointed prattle—
Wives may sew buttons on your shirt,
But yet they’re kittle-cattle.
Tis best to live in single bliss,
In lodgings snug and shady ;
I did not mean to ask my Miss
To be a married lady.
This good resolve I tried to keep
(You see, she hadn’t brothers),
But 0, alas ! 1 fell asleep
One night at Kitty’s mother’s.
■Twas just the chance a woman loves ;
She had been wont to joke me,
And say she’d “ win a pair of gloves ”
Her fond caress awoke me.
I scarce had left the land o’ dreams
Where, facts and fancies mingle;
A kiss at such a moment seems
To make your fingers tingle.
The figure bending o’er my chair
Looked tempting, plump and cosy,
There came a perfume from her hair
As from a summer posy.
I saw a pair of laughing eyes,
A neck as white as lilies,
I seized her hand (the smallest size)
And vowed, like other sillies,
That she was all the world to me—
The rest is easy guessing.
The maiden sat upon my knee
Whilst “ Ma ” bestowed her blessing.
As Adam found before the Ball,
There’s One above disposes
The destiny of each and all,
Though foolish man proposes.
I lull our baby on my lap
To slumber with this ditty ;
My cards are played, I took a nap—
And so I’ve taken Kitty,
E.F.

All the old stage-coach drivers have not
become railway gatekeepers or minor tram officials.
Walter Hall, of Potts Point and Mount Morgan ;
James Rutherford, of Lithgow, Bathurst, and
half-a-dozen other choice spots ; Frank Whitney,
of Coombing Park, Carcoar, the beautiful home
of the Icelys in days gone by, where Sir
Charles Fitzroy, Colonel Mundy, and other
“ bloods ” spent many a roystering evening ; Jacob
Russart, the wealthy host of the Royal Hotel at
Blayney and the local “Dick Whittington;”
“ King ” William Orbell, host of a golden hotel at
Dubbo ; John Fagan, J.P. and squatter, of Cowra
(he drove the coach on the morning Gardiner and
his gang stuck up the escort at Eugowra Rocks);
the late Henry Rotton, of Bathurst (M.L.A.),
and half-a-soore other prominent citizens handled
the ribbons over the Blue Mountains before the
iron-horse found its way westward.

The marine-officers of Victoria want to be
specially represented on the Marine Board. And
why not? Why should the seamen not also be
represented on the Marine Board, in Victoria and
elsewhere? Just now on’y mere wealth—ships
and insurance—are represented. Life and labour
have no special representation. See how the
rotten hulks, the over-laden coffins, are going
down week after week with all hands—purely for
want of seamen’s representation.

Gympie (Q.) people are plain, honest,
folk, and object to their women appearing in
“evening-dress.” The other night one solitary
lady ventured to enter a public hall with half her
vertebree exposed to the wintry blast, when a
strange man loudly offered her his coat, remarking
laconically : “You must be cold.”
# * *
Last week in a perjury case at Carcoar
(N.S.W.) police-court, the accused was sitting on
the right of his solicitor, at whose left stood the
prosecuting attorney. The latter, in addressing
the Bench, referred to “the rogue at the side of
my friend.” “At which side?” enquired the
defending lawyer.
That business-humanitarian organization,
the Co-operative Irrigation and Mercantile
Society of Australia is rapidly becoming a power
among Australian workers, and since the first
intimation of its establishment in The Bulletin,
numbers of disgusted city wage earners have
become settled on lots in one of th > Society’s
colonies. Arrangements have almost been com-
pleted for a fourth colony of magnificent horti-
cultural land in the western district of Victoria,
and within five hours rail of Melbourne, and
shares have been allotted and sold all over this
continent and its islands.*
A N.S. W. Daily Hansard.
Dear Bulletin,— Does not the intense interest shown i n
the Labour members of the N.S.W. Assembly emphasise
the necessity for a daily edition of Hansard? How is it
possible for the democracy to form an intelligent judg-
ment of the Labour members if that judgment is to be
based upon inadequate or inaccurate accounts of the
debates ? True, a weekly, and often a bi-weekly, edition
of Hansard, is published; but this, though excellent in
itself, is only of use for the purposes of record, as public
opinion has already been moulded by the partisan or
crudely condensed reports in the capitalistic press. Is it
not essential, therefore, to the spread of true democracy
that prompt publicity should be given to the whole of the
parliamentary debates ? No person who has not access to
the House can possibly appreciate the deportment of the
Labour members. And, under any circumstances, but a
microscopically small section of the public can be
accommodated in the galleries, owing to want of
room. All chance of misrepresentation of motives
and formation of wrong opinions could be avoided
by the issue every morning of the official report
of the preceding night’s debate. And in this res-
pect New South Wales would be only following the lead of
the Argentine Republic, Belgium, Denmark, Hungary,
Italy, Spain, United States, Queensland, South Australia,
and Canada. No obstacle stands in the way of this demo-
cratic reform but the opposition of officialdom and
capitalism. That a daily Hansard is practicable at a
small additional expense is already admitted. For in 1887
a select committee of the Assembly, of which Mr.
Carruthers was chairman, reported that “it is expedient
to print, publish, and circulate a daily issue of Hansard.”
The motion for the adoption of the report was defeated in
a thin House by 33 to 22; the opposition emanating,
mainly from such representatives of capitalism and class-
dom as Burdekin, Burns, Chapman, Foster, Hawken,
F. Smith, W. Stephen, and other men now rejected
of the people. The difficulty of circulation was
to have been overcome with the aid of one of the
Sydney dailies ; and, speaking on this point, the manager
of the Daily Telegraph said it would cost the colony
£5OOO for printing and circulating the Hansard with any
leading Sydney newspaper “ for a minimum period of
A PIECE OF NEWS.
HE (midship-“mitc”): “You know" we often lave as many as fifty invitations for the
same evening in England, don’tchewno ! ”
SHE (Australian): “Really? I didn’t know they asked the children !”
about four months; with five days sitting each week."
The question is being revived in the Assembly by Mr:
Hutchison, the Opposition member for Glen Innes; and
it is to be hoped that the labour members will support
en bloc a proposition which, while costing but compara-
tively little to the country, will put the people in touch
with the members; will sustain critical interest in all
legislative utterances, will prevent a lot of waste of
time by making reiteration of speeches a thing of the
past, will ensure to the public a full representation of
their views in the Parliament, and will, on the whole,
wonderfully facilitate the business of Parliament. The
circulating of a daily Hansard in leading news-
papers would be of infinite advantage to every
newspaper reader, as it would put the other daily
papers on their mettle, and the competition would lead
to the abolition of the present inadequate, inaccurate and
often partial reports of the press, which now mislead the
public as to the true course of public events, and the
opinions and the tactics of the leading members in the
people’s Chamber. And, if it be deemed expedient, even
a free circulation of a daily Hansard could be obtained
with every metropolitan daily. Nor what is to prevent
the legislature, as a small return for free railway and
postal services by the Slate, from enacting that in every
newspaper published in Sydney there shall be issued, as an
inset or supplement, a copy of the daily Hansard, to be
supwlied by the State printer ?—Yours, Democrat.

Dr. Magee, York Archbishop, died a
pauper, through having to pay £7OOO for the.fees
of translation, Peterborough to York. The Bishop
of Carlisle complains that he had to pay a sine-
cure, named Petty Bag, £l6O for sealing three
documents. The sinecurists, the Barnacles, who
come out of their shells for ecclesiastical jobs, are
named Green Seal, Red Lion, Blue Tiger, and so
on. Even Wales complained to his mother because
Victor was robbed of £4OOO over being made Duke
of Clarence. Magee wouldn’t have minded —if he
hadn’t died.
The Governor of Moscow has consented to
relax the anti-Hebrew decrees so far as to except a
number of Jewish girls, now studying at the
University there, from the general expulsion.
By way of smoothing down the difficulty the
ladies in question have been entered in the police
books as “abandoned women,” and have been
furnished with lodgings in houses of ill-fame.
According to Russian law a fallen woman is not
supposed to be of any religion, therefore she is
exempt from all regulations levelled against any
particular sect. In case, however, the whole of
the Hebrew population should take refuge under
this law it is necessary, as a rule, for the applicant
to satisfy the authorities that her morals are
sufficiently decayed before she can be registered ; if
she doesn’t seem bad enough to fill the bill she
has to go. In the present case, however, the
Governor has kindly guaranteed the bad character
of the applicants.
The Australian Republic conies nearer.
The electors of Wagga vociferously cheered Rae,
M.P., when he called for cheers for the Republic,
and every labour victory shoves Imperialism
deeper down in its grave. The Australian Demo-
cracy will have a hard fight though, and every
man who wants to keep his brain and body in
such trim that all the-most arduous and mental
labours cannot affect them, will have to use the
Bethel Electric Medical Baths, Lonsdale-street,
Melbourne. This particular system of electricity
is a proven cure for digestive, circulatory, nervous
and cutaneous ailments. And that is the reason
why the Imperial Eederationists are looking pur-
blindly towards .
The prospectus of the Sydney Deposit
Bank, whose director is Mr. T. S. Richardson, lies
before us. This institution has introduced a
novelty for public use in the shape of deposit-
notes, which can be purchased by anyone without
giving name or address, while transferrable with-
out endorsement, therefore can be paid away or
given as security, while bearing.interest at a high
rate during the time of currency. These notes are
issued from £lO upwards for any term at the fol-
lowing rates : 12 months, 7 per cent. ; 0 months, 0
par cent. ; 3 months, 5 per cent. But special rates
can be procured for longer or shorter periods.
The Bank on fixed deposits allows for 3 years, !H
percent. ; 12 months, 8g per cent. ; 6 months, 7|
percent. ; 3 months, 5| percent., and during the
last four years a bonus of 1 per cent, has been paid
toiall depositors in addition to the current rates on
their deposits. This is the only Bank in Austra-
lasia.that thus divides its profits.
RHEUMATISM versus MICROBE
KILLER.
294 Castleresgh-street, June 25, 1891.
Radam’s Microbe Killer Co., Gentlemen, —For
forty years I have been a sufferer from rheu-
matism, and latterly in the most aggravated form,
often having acute attacks which made me almost
helpless for days, insomuch that it was with the
greatest pain and difficulty that I could move
about at all, and only able to get up stairs on my
hands and knees. A friend who had tried the
“ Microbe Killer ” with beneficial results sent me
a jar of the medicine, which I took, and I have
now taken three jars of “Microbe Killer” and
the result has been a great wonder to myself and
my friends, for now I can get about freely, with-
out pain. I go up and down stairs without any
trouble, and I am greatly improved in health
every way. In fact my suffering from rheumatism
seems a thing of the past, and I can recommend
the “ Microbe Killer ” to anyone with confidence,
knowing what it has done for me. Several have
tried your medicine on my recommendation for
other diseases—and one a cancer patient—with
the same beneficial results. —Yours respectfully,
MRS. WHITAKER.
Pamphlets sent free. Head office : 40 Elizabeth-
street, Melbourne. Agencies : 319 George-street,
Sydney ; 17 Hunter-street, Newcastle; 69 Queen-
street, Brisbane, etc.
Two Men Wrong-One Man Right.
“ You will he in kingdom-come in less than tivelve
months ! ”
“ You can’t live three years !■ ”
“ You are not going to die ! ”
These three prophecies were addressed to the
same man. The last one had the most comfort in
it, yet he couldn’t tell which had the most inspira-
tion.
Here’s the story. It isn’t pleasant reading at
first, but it “ comes out ” all right, as the children
say.
Perhaps it’s better to let our friend tell it him-
self. We all like the pronoun “I,” that’s why
an autobiography is never dull. This relator is a
butcher of Auckland, N. Z., and his style is so
clear and good, it needs no editing.
He says : “ Some five years ago, when lifting a
quarter of beef, I noticed a remarkable soreness in
the small of my back. I called a doctor, gave up
business, and took to my bed. He prescribed a
lotion to be rubbed in around the region of the
kidneys. This was done, and I remained in bed
several days, suffering excrutiating agony. At
last, having examined the kidney secretions, the
doctor said, ‘ You have Bright’s disease, and will
be in kingdom-come in less than twelve months.’
“ Never believing I had Bright’s disease, I
thought, however, it would be as well to insure
my life. On examination, the company’s physician
refused to pass me for life insurance, saying,
‘ You can’t live three years.’
“ About three months afterwards I was again
seized with a severe attack, and went about more
dead than alive. Finally I broke down and again
took to my bed, as was supposed, for the last
time. I sold one of my businesses so as not to
leave too many affairs for my wife to sunervise,
made my will, and explained to my wife what to
do in case of my death. I can well remember her
listening v ith tears in her eyes to what she be-
lieved were my last instructions. I then lingered
for some weeks, waiting for death to free me from
my sufferings —taking whatever medicines were
prescribed, but deriving little or no relief.
“One evening a friend called, and we talked
over my case and the very serious turn it had
taken. Presently he said, ‘ You are not going to
die. Have you tried Seigel’s Syrup yet ?’
“I confessed that I had not. In fact, I had
religiously followed the instructions of my medical
advisers. My friend persuaded me to try Seigel’s
Syrup, and I began at once, taking twenty-drop
doses according to the directions. In about a
week I felt a little more life in me, and after
having finished the second bottle I was convinced
that improvement had fairly set m. I continued
taking the Syrup until I had consumed ten bottles,
then went downstairs to work again, still using
the Syrup. After having taken from eighteen to
twenty bottles altogether, I found myself entirely
well. It is now four years since I took my last
dose of Mother Seigel’s Syrup for Bright’s disease
of the kidneys, and I have suffered no symptoms
of kidney complaint since.
“(Signed) Robert Hutchinson, Butcher.
Wellington-'street, Auckland, New Zealand.”
We print Mr. Hutchinson’s statement as he
wrote it, yet it is not probable that his malady
was actually Bright’s disease, as that is a degenera-
tion or destruction of the substance of the kidneys,
and difficult, if not impossible, to cure. What he
really suffered from was, no doubt, a debilitated
state of the system, originating in indigestion and
dyspepsia, with severe kidney symptoms, as is often
the case. When we remember that nearly all
maladies, including rheumatism, gout, consump-
tion, liver complaint, heart disease, &o., are due
to poisons in the blocd, arising from fermented
food in the digestive tract, we can see why Mother
Seigel’s Syrup is victorious over so great a variety
of apparently diverse diseases. And because of
the one source of these complaints, serious and
often fatal mistakes are made in the diagnosis and
in the treatment. The point to emphasise is this :
Heart disease, consumption, and Bright’s disease
are much rarer than they are supposed to be.
Generally what seems like them is a group of
symptoms of indigestion and dyspepsia. It is
always best to take the advice of Mr. Hutchinson’s
friend when he said, “ You are not going to die ;
try Seigel's Syrup.”

THE GREAT REMEDY FOR PAIN.
ST.JACOBS OIL.
WHAT IT IS.
SIX STANDARD VIRTUES.
Ist. It is in one word a cure; it is not merely
a relief, and in no sense a cure all ; it is the
product of scientific research.
2nd. It strengthens while it soothes 'and sub-
dues, heals, and cures; it literally conquers pain.
3rd. Its effects are curative and permanent to
the whole group of muscular miseries and nervous
agonies.
4th. It does not merely irritate the outer
surface, nor does it merely soften or relax a con-
stricted muscle. To its specific action a superior
curative virtue is supperadded.
sth. It penetrates deeply but gently; search-
ingly and surely seeking the pain spot in an effort
to conquer.
6th. Each constituent of the formula has a
recognised intrinsic virtue to serve most surely
the cure of pain.
For rheumatism, neuralgia, sprains, bruises,
stiff joints, swellings, and similar ailments, St.
Jacobs Oil has positively no equal if used accord
ing to the directions which accompany each bottle.
Aches and pains succumb in a truly marvellous
manner to the potent influence of this world-famed
remedy. Thousands of testimonials have, from
time to time been published in the Australian
press—testimonials from all ranks and conditions
of men—from statesmen, judges, eminent divines,
barristers, the medical profession, merchants,
athletes, &0., and although a large standing
reward has been offered to any one who could
produce proof that any one of these was not
strictly genuine, none have dared to question
their authenticity. Under these circumstances is
it to be wondered at that the sale of St. Jacobs
Oil greatly exceeds the combined sales of all other
advertised lotions, liniments, embrocations, &c.,
in the world ? No home can afford to be without
a bottle of it always handy, and purchasers would
do well to observe that they get the genuine
article and not one of the many spurious prepara-
tions made up to trade upon the enormous reputa-
tion which St. Jacobs Oil has built up for itself
upon the solid foundation of merit.
ON EXPLOSIONS GENERALLY.
A pre-arranged explosion of anything or any-
body is a tame, insipid, and unpractical method
of attracting attention unless it is carried out on a
very large scale with the usual holiday accompani-
ments of feathers, stars, music, and millinery;
and not always even then unless the unexpected
happens, and something or somebody is blown up
that was intended to remain a fixture either in the
shape of a landmark, or as a permanent ornament
to the pension list of the country. The latter,
however, rarely if ever happens, and when it does
it is sure to be the recipient of a sum of not more
than Is per diem, who goes the highest and has
his remains distributed over the largest part of
the earth’s surface. The first-class pensioner
knows too mujh to incur any needless risk, and is
never in danger of being blown up by anything
more deadly than the internal disturbances pro-
duced by over indulgence in the pleasure of the
table. Of explosive guns that can never be
absolutely depended on to exnlode twice in the
direction of the enemy or any other target, the
colonies have recently had one or two sad ex-
periences, for which somebody might deserve to
lie hanged if it were only possible to get at the
extreme truth; and the same penalty would fit
nicely into the life-mosaic of the genius who not
very long ago invented non-explosive cartridges
filled with sawdust, only on the ground that it
left him a larger margin of profit on his liitie con-
tract. The explosive torpedo, which goes off in
the tube of the attacking ship instead of waiting
until it arrives just underneath the counter of the
foe, and the simple-looking but mature egg which
fulfils its mission in the corner of the eye of the
patriot who is burning to save his country at the
ridiculously low figure of £3OO per annum and a
railway pass, are both equally destructive in
degree, though only one is positively deadly. The
large, angular, carnation-colored iady, with the
gentle-natured mild husband who is always in
the wrong, is an explosive of another type, but
not in the least degree less vicious ; the area
of her iufluence being circumscribed the scatter-
ing force of her fireworks is especially energetic,
and has a most disastrous effect on every living
thing within the range of its baneful influence,
but that range being bounded by the front
door-step on one side and the back gate in
the right-of-way on the other, it has no
particular interest for anybody outside her own
family circle, except for the neighbor’s small boy
who sits on the fence and casually remarks that
“Old man, Johnny, is a ketchin’ of it agin.”
But there is one other domestic explosive that
demands the attention of all Christian people, and
that is low-grade kerosene. It is almost as
impossible to calculate with any degree of certainty
what this will do, or when and how it will do it,
as it is to find out why it does it without any
known or discoverable provocation. One minute
all is peace, comfort, and happiness, and the next
fond hearts have been severed for aye, and time-
payment furniture that it has taken years to
collect is scattered to the four winds of heaven,
and the trusting husband and father comes home
from his work in the evening to find nothing but
a huge chasm in the earth, where he left a happy
home and a united family with a black and gold 1 '
piano and a sewing machine, and a yard full of
poultry in the morning. This has happened,
frequently ; but it need never do so again. The
safeguard against its recurrence is the use of
Luxene, the new illuminant, as a substitute for
deadly oils. Luxene could not bring misery into
the world if it wanted to. Nothing but bliss and
a direct saving of money follows in its track, and
it affords the most soo.hing and beautiful light
known to the wise men and women of to-day.
Make inquiries about it from Messrs. John Barlow
& Co., T, W. Eady & Son, Mason Bros., Ltd., D.
Mitchell & Co., Tillock & Co., J. Keep & Sons,
lloldsworth, MTherson & C,0., M’Lean Bros. &
Rigg, Ltd., and Fred Butcher, Svdney; Jesse
Ireland, R. Hall and Sons, and David Miller,
Newcastle.
