POLITICAL
POINTS
Jhe W.A. Parliament assembles for its
second session in October.
There are 18 more Protectionists than
Freetraders in the N.S.W. Assembly.
Ninian Melville is an aspirant for the
Leadership of the N.S.W. Opposition.
The Victorian railways lose £lOOO a day.
Minister Shiels is apparently the man of the hour.
The Swiss Republic has been established
COO years, and the President’s salary is £6OO a
Parkes, who commenced life as a Chartist,
will be forced to end it as a Socialist. So pro-
phesies The (N.Q.) Republican.
Some members of the N.S.W. Legislative
Prmnoil though not sworn in, still use the railway-
pass and travel “ on the nod ” on the trams.
It was the late Stephen Campbell Brown,
not Thomas Garrett, as asserted who fathered
the £ 40-deposit clause in the N.S.W. Electoral
A °u rpj ie on iy honest Freetrader in the
NSW Parliament is G. H. Reid, who says that
Federation must bring Protection.’’—Miller,
M.L.A. „ , ,
The British fleet actually cost the Aus-
tralia® £14,000 a year more than if it were built-
manned and controlled by Australians as a nur-
sery of Australian sailors.
No wonder Treasurer M £ Millan resigns.
Every nerve and fibre of his being must revolt
against the bearing-rein of the Labour-party and
the temporising of Parkes.
“What the Labour-party is going for is
democratic measures in the interest of Australia,
T wouldn’t care if Beelzebub himself gave
them to us.”—M'Gowan, M.P.
As showing the new (Labour) party’s im-
portance in the N.S.W. Assembly no one now
addresses the chair or the House. They all talk
directly towards and at the Mountain.
Alaiming cable.: £30,000 N.S.W. stock
sold out, because of the local Labour movement—
to be invested, no doubt, in Argentine, Honduras,
Egyptian, Turkish or Portuguese bonds.
°The N.S. Wales Labour-party haven’t
pardoned Parkes. They have only reprieved him.
He is working with a rope round his neck. And
so are Dibbs and Co., if they only knew it.
Thus Samuel Tomkinson, M.P. (S.A.):—
Tie working-classes are not taxpayers, they do not pay
taxes in the ordinary sense. As for Customs duties, the
wages their employers pay them furnish them with the
means.
If any member of the N.S.W. Labour-
party now takes office (and especially if he fails to
put his extra earnings into the Labour Electoral
fund) he will accept a personal bribe pure and
simple.
“ If Mr. Brown had been elected for New-
castle, which he hasn’t, and if I had become
N.S.W. Premier, which I haven’t, Mr. Brown
would have been my Colonial Treasurer, which he
isn’t.”— Dibbs.
Premier Munrope says that “ some of the
Victorian unemployed have been found to be the
owners of freehold property.” The Bulletin
presumes that Munrope refers to the big land-
lords.
Another by-election has been won by the
Gladstonians. The English Tories have notoriously
prolonged the existence of a decaying Parliament
in the hope of wearing out Gladstone’s life before
appealing to the country.
The present utterances of the Australian
Star addressed to the N.S.W. Labour-party fall
on deaf ears and are regarded not as bona-fide
democratic warnings, but as the ravings of politi-
cians hungry for office.
Shoulfl Bilke be elected he will sit as a
Privy Councillor and ex-Oabinet Minister, and
will thus have the right to a place on the front
Opposition bench, shoulder to shoulder with Mr.
Gladstone and Sir William Harcourt.
Mr. E. W. O’Sullivan’s proposal that
Governor Jersey shall summon 10 Labour men to
the N.S.W. Legislative Council is utterly ridicu-
lous so long as no provision is made for the pay-
ment of their Parliamentary expenses.
N.S.W. Speaker Abbott has undergone a
change in his deportment. He is striving, with
some success, to be courteous and considerate to
all members. In the last Parliament he fell under
the impression that he was Parliament.
Lord Chief Justice Coleridge : “ It is not
intimidation to persuade men to leave work.’
Thanks —though we knew it all the time, but
hardly any Australian Judge would have the
hardihood to say it—before an English Judge had
said it.
“ Erak Berry saw clearly enough that if
the Victorian Legislative Council became elective,
on a basis approaching that of the Assembly, it
would claim equal powers. So he was all for a
mere Nominee House, failing which he threw up
the sponge.
The Bulletin again loudly asks the pro-
hibition cranks what they mean to do with the
vineyards of Australia when the State, at their
instance, vetoes the importation or manufacture
of alcoholic liquor? A clear and reassuring answer
on this question is wanted.
The Amalgamated Miners’ Association of
Australia, sitting in conference at Ballarat, have
adopted a political platform, practically that of
the N.S. Wales’ Labour Electoral League. Its
first plank is electoral reform, its last, federation
on the basis of one-man-one-vote.
The Sydney Echo (the S.M. Herald’s
evening pup), in a leaded article, threatens the
Australian labour bodies with Pinkerton guards—
i.e., with military bodies paid by and
used to coerce people who rebel against Free Com-
petition and liberty to starve. We smile.
The election of J. L. Stirling to Adelaide
Legislative Council again shows the power of
labour, although this time defeated. The capi-
talists spent over £2OOO to get their man in, and
the other candidate, a poor man, only came in a
few hundred votes behind him —despite the pro-
perty qualification for voters!
“ If there is any one thing in our whole
social economy that requires sweeping away with
the besom of destruction it is our legal system.
Reform of it is out of the question, for it presents
a stubborn front of resistance, and nothing can be
done with it but batter it down and walk over
it.”—S.M. Herald, 22nd July, 1891.
Apropos the suggestion that Parkes
should appoint one of the Labour members to one
of the two or more vacant portfolios in his Govern-
ment, The Bulletin just wishes to observe that
the Labour-party, as it can do no good with one
or two portfolios, should not accept them. When
the moment comes, they must clutch the lot.
As soon as the Local Government Bill is put
through the N.S.W. Assembly, and the first nail
is driven well home into the political coffins of the
Dowels, the Bowmans, the Dangars, and the
Tonkins, an effort will be made to squelch Parkes’
great vote-controlling machine—the demoralising
paid Parliamentary Public Works Committee.
The total population of Western Australia
on sth April, 1891, was 49.835. The total popula-
tion in 1881 was 29,708. Increase during the
decade, 20,127 —or over 67 per cent. The W.A.
population in 1870 was 24,785, and in 1859, 14,837.
In the census just taken all aboriginals were
rigidly excluded. These are estimated to exceed
5000.
Munro says he will hand over the Vic-
torian unemployed to the Assembly (bound hand
and foot, one presumes), but they will hand them-
selves, and Munro too, over to the next Assembly.
Southey went to a noble lord for a subscription,
and, said the N.L.: “Literature can take care of
itself.” Southey; “Yes, and of you, too.”
The N.S.W. Assembly is likely to sanction
a daily Hansard ; but, to avoid any failures, it
should re-arrange the Government reporting work
so as to confine the Hansard staff to their legiti-
mate duties, which is the reporting of the Parlia-
mentary debates. The present system suits the
comfort, the convenience, and the pocket of only
one man.
The recent statement that Tom Glassey,
Labour M.P., of Queensland, has, since his elec-
tion, got two of his sons into the Queensland Civil
Service, is incorrect. One of the Glasseys entered
the service in ’B5, while his father was not elected
till ’BB. The other was billeted in ’B9 at the enor-
mous salary of 17s. 6d. per week, since increased
to £l.
The House of Lords has ruled that the
fact of men continuing to work at dangerous occu-
pations does not in itself forfeit their rights to
recover damages for injury by accident. This is
a foul blow at “freedom of contract’’—the men
need not work unless they like. They have the
option of starving. The House of Lords has been-
converted into a tyranny.
R. W. Best, M.L.A., of Victoria, recently
essayed to address a meeting of theA.N.A. on
“ Federation,” Premier Munro and Deakin being
present. When Best declared that, iftider the
Convention Bill, the Senate would become Aus-
tralia’s House of Lords, he was howled down for
that evening. Munro got “riled” because the
cat was out of the bag, and Deakin’s face was a
ghastly revelation.
Strenous efforts are being made by the
Victorian white-waistcoat party to defeat Labour
at the general elections. Scene : bank-parlour.
Manager (to clerk): “ How long have you been in
the colony, Mr. Shortage ? ” “Two years, sir. : ’
“Very well. Go and get an electoral right at
once. The bank will bear the expense and will
give you a day’s leave. I hope you will vote
against those who wish to disturb the condition of
society.”
The programme of the Bible in State
Schools’ League formed the text of special sermons
in most of the Queensland Protestant churches
last Sunday. The Protestant churches now find
that they have cut their noses to spite their faces.
With what enthusiasm did they in each province
support the movement for free, secular, and com-
pulsory education out of their hatred for the
Romans ! Now that they try to drive back they
find big trees across the track.
W. A. West-Erskine, who, as per cable,
has been slandering Australia and the Australian
workmen, made a pile out of Broken Hills. This
potentate was for years about the most unpopular
member of S.A. Legislative Council, and resigned
chiefly because he would have been ignominiously
defeated at the recent elections by the very
Labour-party he abuses, notwithstanding the pro-
perty qualification that shuts out half the toilers
from voting.
S. A, Parliament, having affirmed the
principle of eight hours labour, the heads of Civil
Service department were instructed to devise a
scheme for effectuating the system. They now
report that it is impossible for railway engine-
drivers to work exactly eight hours every day, as
they must in certain cases take trains on long
trips. Other absurd arguments are dished up.
These brilliant geniuses don’t understand that all
that Labour asks for is 48 hours a week, parcelled
out in terms as near eight hours as possible, about
which there is no difficulty at all.
Federation literature will receive avaluable
addition —when it suits Parkes’ vanity. He has
had put in type all the despatches that have
passed between Downing-street and Government
House and Hampton Villa; but these important
State papers are being withheld from Parliament
—until Parkes’ literary trumpeter has exhausted
their contents in the interest of an edition-de-luxc
of the Convention records, in which his Federa-
tion labours will be extolled as highly as it will be
seemly for a literary Civil servant to beslobber his
political patron. Will the N.S.W. Assembly con-
sider the Commonwealth Bill in the absence of
these public, not Parkesian, papers ?
Arthur Rutledge, M.L.A. (Q.), favours
the proposed second-ballot system for the extra-
ordinary reason that its adoption in France and
Germany had “enabled those countries to stem
the tide of political socialism which otherwise
would have inundated them. ” This is about the
blindest attempt at seeing nothing ex-parson and
lawyer Rutledge has ever made. France is
solidly Republican, while Germany hasn’t yet
stemmed any tide worth mentioning, except that
of human blood, and is as honeycombed with
socialism —even in its Parliament—as Rutledge’s
skull is with fallacies. All the “stemming” (of
the Rutledge brand) ever yet done anywhere has
been effected by the injustice of plural voting.
There are more Socialists in Germany than in any
other country in the world, and in France the ten-
dency towards Communistic-Anarchy is equally
strong.

Personal Items.
Hlx-Mayor Burdekin’s income is £lO per
hour.
Chief - Justice Higinbotham has never
allowed himself to be nominated a member of any
club.
They say of a certain M.L.A. that he
was first a sundowner, then a burntdowner, now
he’s a rundowner.
Walking Sydney streets in a most dilapi-
dated condition is a grandson of the sweet-singing
Bishoo Heber, of “ India’s Coral Strand.”
At Sydney last week, in the eternal fitness
of things, Mr. Lamb married Miss Mutton-
Happy woman, thus to renew her youth 1
Parnell has not yet paid the £lOOO costs
in his divorce-case. When he does he will pro-
bably enter.it in the ledger to “wedding-expenses.”
The finest vocalist among Australian clergy-
men is Canon Tucker, of South Yarra, Melb. His
“Cujus Animam” almost excites professional
envy.
It was upon the advice of the notorious
James Davis, erstwhile editor of The Bat, that
Gordon-Cumming took legal proceedings against
the Wilsons.
Treasurer M'Millan’s mother has founded,
at her own expense, a home where the daughters
of deceased Irish Wesleyan ministers may reside
free of charge.
Giving evidence in the baccarat-case, the
Prince of Wales spoke more like a German than
an Englishman. He even said “Lorf’when he
meant “ Lord.”
Labouchere’s defence of Gumming, in
Truth, should be read by everybody. It is con-
vincing enough to gain for the defeated baronet
the verdict of public opinion.
It is stated that Rudyard Kipling’s grand-
fathers were both Wesleyan ministers. It is also
stated that Rudyard has now abcesses in both
lungs and is simply dying of consumption.
A hairless man died up in Deniliquin
(N.S.W.) the other day. He had never gazed on
the bal'et in Ids 1 ifo, but had a red wig built for
him by public subscription every few years.
John Hurley declares that the utterances
of Judge Harding in the Rockhampton “conspir-
acy cases are worthy of being engraven in imper-
ishable characters.”” Yes, on the red-hot walls of
Sheol.
A Paris paper estimates at 6,500,000fr5.
(£200,000) the amount which Bernhardt has earned
since her debut in 1807. Her present tour, which
is to bring £BO,OOO into her coffers, is counted in
the total.
Of the Victorian Legislative Assembly of
1802, of which the just-deceased B. G. Davies
was a member, but five now remain--L. L. and
W. C Smith, Duncan Gillies, William M’Lellan
and John Woods.

bane, Q. This, we believe, is the gentleman who
suffered by the second shot of the maniac U'Farrell
at Clontarf, the shot, by the way, that Sir William
Manning “ went down to avoid.”
Young Longstafi’e, the Victorian artist,
got an official honourable mention for his picture
in the Salon. Phil. May writes saying that Long-
staffe is “a real clinker,” but adds that he is
having a big struggle as he is not well off.
After the verdict which condemned
Gordon - Gumming, Miss Garner, the semi-
millionairess who is now his wife, was asked
whether she intended to marry or to keep her
liberty. She replied : “ I intend to do both.”
The S.M. Herald’s latest London corres-
pondent is the well-known and well-informed
Henry W. Lucy, late of London Daily News—a
perfect marvel in point of industry, who ha« been
seen standing up and dictating two articles at the
same time.
Bompas, Q.C., Recorder of Plymouth,
who recently gave the notorious decision that ask-
ing a man under engagement to leave his work in
itself constituted legal “ intimidation,” is the son
of Serjeant Bompas, the original of Dickens’
Serjeant Buzfuz.
Under the heading, “Illness of Great
Men,” Melbourne Standard announced that
W. H. Smith, M.P., was recovering from his
recent indisposition. The information that old
Bookstall Smith is a great man comes upon us
like a thunderclap.
When a certain Melbourne millionaire
walked out of his shop the other day, having just
sold his business for £300,000 odd, he neither
shook hands with, nor said goodbye to, any of his
men, some of whom had been serving him for
thirty long and weary years.
Edward Giles, sergeant - major of the
“gold police,” who arrested the clever swindler
known as “Lord Lascelles” in the thirties, still
lives on the Turon river, N.S.W., in the enjoy-
ment of his pension. With one exception he is
the oldest police pensioner, having retired from
the service in 1859.
Gerald O'Shea, Captain O’Shea’s eldest
son, who gave evidence in the divorce-trial, and
talked about kicking “that awful scoundrel
Parnell ” has now gone over to his mother’s camp.
This is regarded as symptomatic of a depression
in the chances of Captain O’Shea in the prospects
of the impending will-suit.
Alfred Deakin’s declaration that he is
quite a poor man, working hard for his daily
bread, doesn’t fit in with the oft-repeated but
perhaps apocryphal story that David Syme
recently offered him a partnership in the Age on
long terms. Why, Alfred could have sold the
“long terms.” to a syndicate for a fat round sum.
A bronze statue of Peter Lalor will be
fixed up in Sturt-street, Ballarat, by the end of
the year. Contrary to first report, this worthy
effigy of the Eureka hero makes a feature of the
gap where his arm should have been. Even in
his Speaker’s robes (not half as gaudy as Davies’,
by the way), Peter will be recognised as chief of
the Eoys of the Old Stockade.
Dean O’Hea, the retired and wealthy
R.C. Melbourne cleric, was journeying one day
witli an old churn, a Presbyterian parson named
M'Leod, with whom he engaged in polemics.
“Now,” said Mac, “I can see no difference
between the Virgin and my mother—both were
good women.” “ Aye,” quoth the “ Dane, “but
there is a lot of difference betune their sons.”
Old Henderson Africanus was tried in
Melbourne some years ago on a charge of stabbing
somebody and acquitted. The first thing Hender-
son did when liberated was to go down to the
Herald office and buy up nearly the whole of the
late edition, which ho hawked round the suburbs,
yelling, “Evenin’ ’Erald, latest edition, full
account of the trial of Henderson Africanus ! ”
The Maoriland R.C. Primate, Dr. Red-
wood, runs a paper which, during the strike,
vigorously championed the ship-owners against
the men ; but in consequence of the tenor of the
Pope’s encyclical, he has had to turn right-about-
face. The cream of the joke is that the bishop’s
naper is edited by a clever Bohemian, a born
humourist, chiefly known to fame as a former
Atheistic lecturer.

The following testimonials from ministers, doctors, and private persons speak for them-
selves and they are but a drop in the bucket of the thousands received :
The distinguished pulpit-orator and evangelist,
Rev, Miles Grant, Boston, Mass., writes,
October 7; , .
“ *** Touching your wonderful discovery, I take much
pleasure in saying, “I am delighted with its effects upon
my system. I sent for it that I might know its influence
on a healthy person. I have now used it about three
months, and intend to continue its use during my mortal
life. The treatment produces a general refreshing, in-
vigorating effect; sharpens my appetite, sweetens my
sleep, and makes me feel as though I had a new lease of
life. I have persuaded several of my brethren of the
ministry to send for your Health-Pamphlet, and so far as
I have heard from them they are exceedingly pleased.
One of them said to me a few days ago, ‘ I would not
take $5O for it. It knocks the bottom out of disease. I
can say for myself I never spent cash to better advantage
than when I bought your pamphlet. —Your brother in
'Christ, Miles Grant.”
Dr. J, W. Graves, Parsons, Kansas, writes :
“Dear Dr. Hall,— After a careful reading of your
pamphlet, which came to hand a few weeks ago, I became
so delighted with the far-reaching effects of its marvellous
physiological revelations, that I at once ordered six of my
patients, who were suffering with consumption, to com-
mence the use of the treatment. lam happy to say that
they are all improving and doing well. You have done a
great work for afflicted humanity.—Your fraternal friend,
J. W. Graves, M.D.”
Rev. Thos. Nield, Livonia, Ind., writes :
“At the time I received your pamphlet on Health and
Longevity, I was suffering from internal piles. After
putting your treatment into practice three times I bade
my trouble good-b3'e, and have not felt it since. I have
only to add, from practical experience, during the short
time I have owned your pamphlet, that 1 have more faith
in your system of treatment than in all the medicines of
all the doctors on earth combined. —Yours very truly,
Thos. Nield.”
Rev. A. L. Cole, D.D., M.D., Santa Ana, Cal.,
sends his second endorsement of the new pam-
phlet after another month’s practice of the treat-
ment, as follows;
“ Dear Dr. Hall,— l have nothing to take back from
my letter as printed in the July ‘Microcosm.’ Your
treatment is the first expression of the science of thera-
peutics I have ever seen in print, or have ever put
into practice that appeals to my judgment as unquali-
fiedly rational. It goes directly to the cause of all forms
of disease and removes it by aiding and abetting nature,
rather than provoking a conflict among the vital powers
as in the case of drug-medication. This I call genuine
remedial science. For nearly twenty-five years I have
been a consecutively tormented invalid, and I can say in
all candour and truth that the first real freedom from suf-
fering that I have experienced in all this time has been
since adopting your treatment. I rejoice beyond words
within my power of expression ; and I honestly believe
that under God you have done more in that pamphlet
than all the doctors of the world for the relief of suffering
humanity. The treatment in my own case is to me the
wonder of wonders ! The $4 I paid for the pamphlet was
the best spent money of my whole life. You are at liberty
to print this, which I declare to be the literal truth upon
the honour of a man and a Christian. God bless you in
your beneficent work.”—A. L. Cole.
Rev. Geo. Cook, of Millican, Texas, who was

three more pamphlets with pledges signed My health is

pamphlet are just beginning to try the treatment, and
alLare pleased with it. I will report further soon, and
send for a number of pamphlets.—Yours truly, George
Cook."
Let it be distinctly understood that this is no
scheme of quackery to draw money out of the
afflicted, nor is it any sort of a patent medicine
humbug, since there is not a grain of medicine of any
kind required in the treatment. Notwithstanding
this fact, it takes directly hold of the worst cases
of constipation, dyspepsia, liver complaints, head-
aches, heart disease, incipient consumption,
diabetes, and Bright’s disease of the kidneys, in-
cluding fevers and inflammation of the lungs, and
of other internal organs—not by attacking these
so-called diseases directly, but by radically neutra-
lising and removing their causes, thus allowing
nature herself to do her work unimpeded.
We now simply repeat that the new treatment
acts so radically and fundamentally upon the vital
circulation without the least danger to the most
delicate constitution, that there is not a form of
disease that flesh is heir to, which will not be
affected favorably to the patient by an intelligent
use of this method of treatment.
In selling this Pamphlet we are obliged to re-
require a “ Pledge of Honor ” from those purchas-
ing it, not to show or reveal its contents outside
of their own families. This is for our own protec-
tion, and as the only means by which our just
rights in the discovery can be ma’ntained.
Doctors, of course, who purchase are not debarred
from using the treatment in their own practice,
providing they do not show the pamphlet or do not
reveal the rationale of the treatment.

DR. R. F. STEVENS’INDORSEMENT,
Dr. Stevens, of Syracuse, the first
M.D. to whom we revealed the new treatment—
more than 20 years ago. In writing to a friend,
about a year ago, he gives the following
***** “Nearly twenty years ago in this city Dr. A.
Wilford Hall gave me privately a detailed history of a
hygienic treatment he had discovered and adopted some
twenty years before, and which he had practised con-
tinuously upon himself up to that time. The history
included an account of the failure of the health, the
reasonings and conclusions which led him to adopt the
treatment, his rapid restoration to health, &c. From the
time of his first mention of it to me I have been entirely
satisfied that it saved him from a lingering consumption
and death ; and that in the years following, as I have evi-
dence to satisfy me, its continued practice has so affected
the nutrient and eliminating functions as to give to his
physical structure a greater degree of solidity, or, in
other words, a higher specific gravity than that possessed
by any other man living. I learn that he has persistent!v
continued the treatment to the present time-forty years
in all—and that his present health and vigorous condition
may rightfully be compared to the ‘ bloom of youth,’
though he is nearly seventy years of age. The tests I
have made of the treatment upon myself and in ray prac-
tice in the twenty years past, have convinced me that
great practical benefit may be derived by members of the
medical profession and others from a careful study of the
rationale of the treatment as discovered by Dr. Hall, the
details of which he proposes to give in his forthcoming
book. A debt of gratitude will surely be due to him for
whatever scientific advances he may be able to unfold in
the line of assisting nature to ward off disease, and in the
restoration of health.— R. F. 'Stevens, M.D.”

“Sent to Australia.”
Dear Bulletin, —In a recent issue of The Bul.
letin was a paragraph of two lines only. It
referred to some poor wretch who was being sent
to Australia for his health. He was suffering
from consumption. That was all, and to the
casual reader conveyed nothing more than the
fact stated. But to me it had more than a pass-
ing interest, for it was but one example of ten
thousand others, each one a tragedy in itself.
# # ' *
Ten years ago I came in a sailing-ship bound for
Australia. We had twelve passengers on board,
six of these in more or less advanced stages of
consumption. They all told pretty much the
same tale. Poor clerks or mechanics, Algiers
or any other resort of wealthy consumptives
was impossible for them. They must go where
t hey could earn their living, so they had been sent
to Australia to earn their living—if they could,
and recover —if possible. To each one, also, hi 8
medical adviser had said, “Go by sailing-ship,
the long sea-voyage will be so pleasant—and it is
cheaper.”
# # *
We were becalmed for a week in the doldrums,
and the moist heat of the Equator killed the
weakest of the six. In the high latitudes of the
Indian Ocean, in the region of the icebergs, there
was no more heat, but there was cold enough and
to spare. Day after day there were storms of
snow and heavy gales, the waves pouring over the
sides and washing the passengers out of thei r
cabins on deck. We were two months beating
from the Cape to Sydney, and in that time we
buried three more. The other two walked ashor e
to earn their living, for perhaps three months.
After that they would have no more need to
trouble.
* # *
A short time back I was sitting one evening in
the surgeon’s cabin of a mail-steamer. We were
coming down the Mediterranean, the night wa s
fine and it was the hour of tobacco and whisky
and soothing thoughts, but the surgeon was
cursing—for the want of a better simile —like any-
thing. He cursed the ship, and he cursed the
passengers, he cursed the directors and he cursed
his profession, and finally exhausted himself in
one almost inarticulate curse against the combined
medical faculty of Great Britain. Then, some-
what calmer, he stated his grievance. It was his
fourth voyage, and always the same experience.
So many consumptives in the second - class,
so many in the steerage sent to Australia
to earn their living—or die. “ It is not as
though it did them any good,” he continued, “or
I wouldn’t mind, but the sea voyage can’t—the
changes of climate are too great and too sudden.
You know that it can’t, and I know that it can’t,
and dozens of others know the same. It is the
blithering idiots at home who send them out to
get rid of them, that don’t know and won’t learn.
We lost three last trip, and I guess we shall lose
four this.” We were luckier than this, however,
for we only buried two, and the rest were landed
to add to the strength and sinew of the popula-
tion—while they lived.
# * *
So much for the sea-voyage, but how about
those who survive it ?
* # *
I was riding one morning over the Downs in
Western Queensland. I had started early, bound
for a neighbouring station, and although well
within the tropics it was bitterly cold. There
had been a sharp frost at sunrise, and crossing the
ti-tree gullies a thick mist hung about that pierced
through to the very bones. Presently I overtook
a horseman. The leather leggings and slouch hat
proclaimed the jackeroo, the seat denoted the
new-chum. Riding side by side—for we were
bound for the same destination—l took stock of
my companion. Thin and haggard there was no
doubt about his case—the hacking cough, which
the bitter morning air brought on in paroxysms,
betrayed that plainly enough. He told me his
story. A clerk in London, sent out by the doctor
with a letter of introduction to a Melbourne
firm, passed on by them, as bookkeeper, to North
Queensland, “where it was always fine and
warm.” “There was ice in the hut last night,”
he continued, “ and it is not very warm now, but
I daresay I shall be better when the summer
comes.”
* * *
After breakfast I asked the manager at M
about his bookkeeper. For answer he took me
round to a small enclosure away behind the
station. In the enclosure there were three slabs
marked each one by a plain ironbark slab.
“Those,” he said, “ are the graves of three of my
bookkeepers, sent out from home for the sake of
the voyage, and sent up here for the sake of the
climate; the present man will be the fourth, and
after that, I’ll be d——d if I stand it any longer.
The blank fools at home and the blanker fools in
Melbourne don’t know what the climate is for a
man who has to work. They don’t turn this
station into a blank asylum for consumptives any
more. I’ll be getting the complaint myself
directly. ” When I passed M— — again, fourteen
weeks later, the fourth was there. And yet this
one had a better chance than most.
* * #
Now for the moral of all this. Year after year
shipload after shipload of pauper and quasi-pauper
consumptives are being brought to our shores. It
can do them no good, and it can do us no good,
and the only good it can do is to the (on this
point) ignorant fools who send them away to save
themselves further trouble. To the consumptive
of wealth Australia affords a climate where, with
suitable precaution in certain particular localities,
an absolute prolongation of life may be obtained ;
to the man who must work for his living, handi-
capped by a mortal disease, the climate is more
than usually fatal. The extremes of heat and
cold are greater than he has been accustomed to,
the life in the country where the consumptive
usually makes his way is one of greater privation,
and a speedier termination of the disease is the
inevitable result. Of the thousands that have
come here, there is probably not one single case
of established consumption where the slightest
benefit has accrued. And yet, to obtain this
absolutely negative result, what are the risks that
we take? Many bring children, the hereditary
poison already within them, to fructify in after
years. Many more have time to marry and have
children born to them ere they die, leaving a legacy
of disease behind them. There is already too great
a tendency to indigenous consumption among
many native-born Australians, that we should add
to the mischief by the gratuitous importation of
billions of tubercle germs'. Imbecile authorities
who become hysterically frantic over the possible
introduction of the mildest case of chicken-pox
should be forced to protect the people of the Aus-
tralias against the further introduction of the
most easily-detected and most dangerous disease
of all. —Yours, &c., George Dant.
A Generous Gift.
It matters not under what conditions of life we
are placed, in poverty or in wealth, there are
times when we look round for the means' to “get
away from ourselves.” This feeling may arise
through a desire to blot out for a time bid recol-
lections, to forget the “ skeleton in the cupboard,”
or to deaden passing troubles. With this aim in
view many means are resorted to which only too
frequently end in insanity and death. Amongst
these may be mentioned alcohol, tobacco, opium,,
henbane, hachish and absinthe, about which so
many terrible stories are told. Happily, however,
although the desire cannot be suppressed, the
right-thinking amongst us seek to satisfy our-
selves in a less harmful manner. In which
direction the patronage accorded to our local
theatres clearly shows. Music and the drama,
above everything else, create a feeling of satis-
faction and a much sought after forgetfulness of
self thatnothing else can approach, setting asidethe
permanent educational advantages that it must
also necessarily give. And yet how few there are
amongst us who, sitting night after night in our
box' or comfortably-cushioned dress-circle seat,
give five minutes’ thought after the performance
to those who so patiently administer to our plea-
sure and who so materially help us to forget the
troubles which are the penalty of our existence.
The present, much less the future, of the actors
and musicians are entirely lost sight of in our own
immediate thought of self, and yet no one denies
the hardships of their lives. During a recent
visit paid to Dunwich Asylum, Mr. Henry J.
Pollard, of the firm of H. J. Pollard and Co., who
is really one of the pioneers of music in Queens,
land, was surprised and grieved to find so many
old professionals, who had seen life in all its
phases, ending their days in almost cheerless soli-
tude. Born and educated in an atmosphere of
harmony and song, and in full possession of all
their musical faculties, they were totally without
the means of enjoying a pleasure they had
spent their best years in giving to us. Such is the
irony of fate, and so it struck Mr. Pollard
as he thought upon the happier and more
prosperous circumstances under which he had seen
them. Mr. Pollard’s visit to Dunwich has conse.
quently not been without its good results, for we
are informed that with the permission of the
Colonial Secretary he has presented the inmates
of that institution with a magnificent Hermann
Schroder piano. We do not wish to make any
comment on this generous gift, the spirit in which
it has been given speaks for itself. We announce
it in this public manner only with the hope that the
many visits paid during the year to this haven of
rest (to which anyone of us may come, and which
even now is filled up with old acquaintances) may
create in many the same emotions, and be pro-
ductive of the same good. We trust our few
remarks on Mr. Pollard’s generosity may not fall
upon unwilling ears, but may open the hearts of
the charitably-disposed to assist in “toning
down ” the evening of life for the “old boys” of
Dunwich.*
Bernhardt’s wonderful vitality can be
traced to one or two things. Either she has a
small electric battery instead of a heart, or she
paid fortnightly visits to the Bethel Electric
Medical Baths at Lonsdale-street during the cur-
rency of her Melbourne season.
J. and C. Howard, of Pitt-street, Sydney,
are the Australasian representatives of J. and F.
Howard Bedford, so well-known to agriculturists.
After some years of hard work, they have won
very important distinctions for many of their
manufactures such as have been deemed particu-
larly adapted for colonial use. Among these are
their “DiggingPloughs.” Now, practical plough-
men, everywhere, have had to withdraw their pre-
judices against the concave mouldboard and
broken work. The peculiar shape of the mould -
board enables it to pulverise the soil, and the
presser, a projection from the end of the
board, aiding in this work, also helps to
level the ploughed land and lay a good
seed bed. The metal of this board is the
hardest ever made, and has a gloss which
means greater cleaning power than every
other. The wings, or shares, of these ploughs are
fitted with reversible points of several patterns—
patents of the firm—which can be reversed in
order to allow the cutting edge to be always at the
bottom, and, when worn out, can be replaced for
half the cost of ordinary plough-shares. A small
mould-board and share placed in front of the
other effectually buries all weeds and rubbish.
The single-furrow digging ploughs are now fitted
with a large skeith coulter, which is intended to
out cornstalks previous to being buried by the
plough. This plan seems to have solved the great
difficulty of maize-farming. The Agricultural
College has been supplied recently with these
digging ploughs after an exhaustive trial. Messrs.
Howard’s safety enclosed horse-gears and patent
radial rising mouthpiece chaff-cutters are now
becoming well known and liked for their impor-
tant meehanical points. We advise our readers
to become conversant with modern implements of
agriculture by sending for the firm’s new cata-
logue—a valuable addition to the literature of any
farmer.

Faithful “Towzer.”
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
The night was dark, His coat, behind.
Hidden each star ; The thief in vain
The watch-dog’s bark Strives to get free;
Was heard afar. He suffers pain,
The watchman’s tread Captivity.
Resounded plain ; A woeful cry
From overhead From the poor wight
Down poured the rain. Is raised on high
With head bowed down, At each fresh bite.
Soft-footed crept
Towards the town At last, a light
A thief adept. Is seen appear;
The watch-dog’s bark In garments slight
Is hushed and gone— A man draws near.
On mission dark Ho calls aloud
The thief creeps on, To one inside ;
But “ Towzer ” sniffs Another shroud
The danger near, Is seen to glide
And quickly lifts Adown the stairs—
A wakeful ear. O'er the grass-plot.
His eyes gleam bright This figure bears
With thoughts of beef Poker, red-hot.
Which he shall bite The fearful brand
From out that thief. Is quick applied
Where “Towzer” and
Soft on the wall The thief collide.
The thief has dropped ; There comes a yell
The heavy fall Of anguish fresh ;
Of rain has stopped. A nasty smell
The lightning’s flash Of burning flesh.
To him displays One mighty pull;
A window-sash The thief is freed 1
Easy to raise. Dog's mouth is full—
With gentle push A glorious feed.
It rises high;
When, lo ! a rush—
A fearful cry In pain, alone,
Heard miles at least! Sure-footed crept,
A growl of rage, On his way home,
And man and beast That thief adept.
A warfare wage. Good “ Towzer ” nods,
Good “Towzers’ ” teeth And dreams of beef,
A firm hold find, And prays the gods
Just underneath To send more thief.
KOHIMANA.
Somebody has been trading off artificial
Kings of Egypt, made out of donkey-skin, to
museums all over Europe, and there is a consider-
able row and riot in scientific circles as a con-
sequence. The process of making a King is very
simple. Take one old and hide-bound ass, and
wallop the animal to make his cuticle more pliable.
Then kill him, and sell his tail for ox-tail' soup.
Cut his skin into a sort of human shape and fit it
neatly on a lay-figure; then sew it together, and
stuff it with spice and ashes. Write some
hieroglyphics on the stomach setting forth that
this was the monarch who traded off corn at a
high figure during the dry spell in ancient Egypt,
and there you are. The King and donkey are so
wonderfully alike that no one can tell the differ-
ence.
Francisque Sarcey, in Scribner, for last
month, June, touches up Sara Bernhardt, and
here is how he tells the story. During a “Erou-
Frou ” rehearsal somebody said Sara was very
exacting ;
Sarah remained pensive, bub she smiled and said : “ It
is true, lam very exacting ! ” Then suddenly, becoming
serious; ■
“ Yes, it is all very fine ! But the end? Ah ! the end !
The thing is to end well! The climax should be dramatic
and stirring ! Suppose that Rochefort —whose death Ido
not wish, understand me—had been killed by a bullet at
the moment of his escape ! What an admirable death !
There is a climax ! A fine fifth apt! I should like to end
that way 1 Gambetta ended well'—-drama, mystery.
Come, tell me, how do you think I shall end?”
Nobody answered.
There were many pensies de derriere la tgte, to use
Sainte-Beuve’s expression, in the glances that were ex-
changed behind Sara, close to the side scene. Then a
very young comedian, almost a supernumerary, who
played in “ Frou Frou ” an insignificant role , that of a
domestic, shrugged his shoulders and answered his
directress— his directress !—with the thick and mocking
accent of the Paris street-boys.
‘‘You? How you will end, you? It isn’t hard to
guess ! You will end as a box-bperier!”
And do you think that Sara got angry with her pupil ?
Well, I should say so! She burst into laughter. She
found the answer so amusing. Box opener! . . . .
Gavroche, va ! She must have told the story herself.
She was always ready to laugh at everything. In her
dressing-room, the money which she received daily
evaporated like gold dissolved by aqua regia. There
went on, between the acts, on the nail, as it were, a daily
distribution of her salary by fractions, by hundreds and
twenties of francs. Her fifteen hundred francs would be
brought to her. Quick the pillage, the division, the
quarry! Poor woman: . . . “This for you, Madame,
G 1 A bouquet to be paid for. Good! . . .
Here ! take this, you ! Carry it to the hair-dresser ! Ah !
an instalment to X '. . . . So much to Z 1
. . . Good! . . . What else ? . . . T has
written this morning. I send him this, he’ll have to be
patient.” . . . And still laughing: “ What is left me
now ? Fifteen francs ! Bah ! with fifteen francs one need
not starve ! Rut get change for this five-franc piece, I
need it for the carriage 1”
And a similar scene was enacted almost nightly in
Sara’s dressing-room.

Said Bishop Magee : “I once heard of an
ill-paid minister who went to his deacon to solicit
an increase of salary. “ Salary ! ’ said the deacon,
“ I thought you worked for souls ? ” “So I do,”
replied the poor man, “but I cannot eat souls;

your size to make a dish ! ”
According to Geraldton (W.A.) Advo-
cate, 18 mechanics at a local plantation refused
to work, st<ting that they could not work on the
breakfast offered them. They complain that the
ration male was so poor that frequently the men
who were last in re ponding to the meal-call got
nothing, and had to rely on their mates’ good
nature. They got no bedding nor light, and no
soap to wash themselves, and were penned up in
quarters not fit for pigs.

A BOON TO HUMANITY
Tis food for young and food tor old,
rnj gives new life and vital power,
-mi soothing in the market sold
uke Pemell’s patent Snowwiutk Flour.
Word of Mystery [-Grotesque !
Tanglefootish [—Arabesque!
Thou art, one sees at a glance,
Bursting with significance,
Wild and strange, and weird and mystic,
Theosophies cabalistic;
Gay, perhaps, or, p’raps terrific,
Potent, pregnant, Hieroglyphic!
Lo ire hail thee, with loud lung,
To'the freedom of the tongue !
Take thy place— thy place of pride—
Close to Jimjams—side by side !
Clever lady ! Skilful, rare,
Marvellous interpreter !
Who but thee a thought could strike
’Neath that utt’rance Stygian-like ?
Who but thee could, at a spring.
Know that “Sigglum” meant -The Thing?
Jove! Thy intuition deep
Almost makes the flesh to creep.
To the tongue Bohemian,
Jargon of the cup and can,
Thou a Benefactress art,
After its (and our) own heart.
From this forth—from this same hour—
Husbands have a word of pow’r,
When, at grey of dawn, they tumble
Homeward, hatless, hopeless, humble,
Flapping-armed and knooking-kneed,
From a testimonial Feed ;
And whore, in a twist, they fall 1
On the mat within the hall,
And their wives, with wrath kept hot,
Come to cut the Gordian knot
With the axe-shaft—they must square
Up, and fix her with a glare,
And, without a thought of fear,
Hiccough “Sigglum ” in her ear.
She will not suspect the dodge,
But believe they’ve been at Lodge,
And that—innocence divine !
“ Sigglum ” was the countersign.
Word of pow’r for others, too,)
When their mouths are full of glue,
And, in some strange suburb, they
On the Sabbath—arid day !
Wander, while they think each pub.
Locked up with the lock of Chubb,
Let them bend down and with speed
(Better day the better deed !)
Whisper in the keyhole—quick—
“ Sigglum V'—that will do the trick.
Carried by a powerful breath,
“Sigglum ” is the shibboleth
That hath power to control
All true Brothers of the Bowl.
Ecce Sigglum ! When the stout
Tanglefooters’ men march out,
In the pomp of their array,
Proud of heart on Eight Hours’ Day,
What should be their sign, we ask,
But a bottle, or a cask?
And what motto full of force
Should be writ thereon ? Of course—
Meaning all to it must stoop—
“ln hoc Sigglum Vinces ” whoop !

The Lost Drink
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
I had spent the night in the watch-house —
My head was the size of three—
So I went and asked the chemist
To fix up a drink for me;
And he brewed it from various bottles
With soda and plenty of ice,
With something that smelt like lemon,
And something that seemed like spice.
It fell on my parching palate
Like the dew on the sun-baked plain,
And my system began to flourish
Like the grass in the soft spring rain;
It wandered throughout my being,
Suffusing my soul with rest,
And I felt as I “ scoffed ” that liquid
That life had a new-found zest.
I have been on the razzle-dazzle
Full many a time since then,
But I never could get the chemist
1 To brew me that drink again.
He says he’s forgotten the notion —
’Twas only by chance it came—
He tried me with various liquids,
But oh ! they are not the same.
We have sought, but we sought it vainly,
That one lost drink divine ;
We have sampled his various bottles,
But somehow they don’t combine :
Yet I know when I cross the River
And stand on the Golden Shore
I shill meet with an angel-chemist
To brew me that drink once more.
B.
A “ Native Land Laws Commission ”
which has been travelling Maoriland, taking
evidence from Maori and Pakeha anent _ land
transactions has unearthed some lively stories of
old times and of other times not very remote.
One of them relates to the last Maori war. New
Zealand’s “only general,” and a K.C.M.G. at
that, was one day lying in his tent when he heard
a Maori outside having an altercation with the
sentry. “Oh, never mind, allyright,” said the
Maori “me leave ’em here to-night, (men-
tioning the General’s name) give me the utu in
morning,” and with that the flap of the tent was
lifted and 40 gory heads were rolled in on the
supposed sleeper. The Government gave £5 a
head and “no questions asked” in those days,
and this industrious native collector had by dint
of patience, frugality, and hard work accumulated
£2OO worth of stock. The General won’t listen
to this story now, and it is not to appear in his
forthcoming book, but when it was recently told
in his presence and he strongly repudiated it,
another eminent colonial officer slapped him on
the back and said “ that’s all right old boy, we
know all about it, but it’s nothing to be ashamed
of —why we gave £lO a head up our way ! ” So
they did, and some awkward mistakes the too
covetous collector made sometimes. Another
story unearthed by the Commissioners related to
“ Makutu.’ To “ Makutu ” anyone is to bewitch
them so that they die—what an Irishman would
call “ putting the comether on ’em.” It is a fixed
article of belief amongst'the Maories, and a man
or woman who believe he or she is “Makutued ” will
calmly sit down and die, and then after decently
“ waking ” the corpse at a “ tangi,” the relatives
go round and look for the witch, and having found
him or her, promptly knock them on the head as
a precaution against their practising Makutu on
anyone else. There have been countless instances
of this sort of thing, some of them within the
last few years and only the other day an old fellow
was tried for witchcraft with his life as the
stake. It was a convenient way of getting
obnoxious folk out of the road and is an interesting
survival of “ the good old times.” The “ Makutu ’
that we refer to didn’t happen well for the plain-
tiff’s counsel. It was during the height of the
“Pai Marire” when the tohungas taught their
followers that they were bullet-proof and that
they had only to utter certain incantations to
eternally blast the presumptuous pakeha. An
old fellow named McMillan—Finlay McMillan—
lived in a native district. He had expressed him-
self contemptuously of the Pai Marire, and in
return the Hauhaus promised to put the
“comether ” over him. One day he was walking
alone along a lonely beach when he suddenly came
upon a Pai Marire fishing party. He was un-
armed, and so close that escape was hopeless.
With the party was an old priestess of powerful
“ mana”and fixing a fine rolling eye on Finlay,
who cordially wished he was home with his ma,
she commenced to roll out an incantation that
was to paralyse him in one act. By this time
Finlay was surrounded by enthusiastic Hauhaus
who squatted on the sand and fingered their
tomahawks lovingly in preparation for giving
practical effect to the curse if it didn’t do its work
with the expected neatness and despatch. Finlay
was greatly troubled and felt something more
than dead long ere the priestess began to get blue
in the face with her exertions and long after the
pupils of her eyes had vanished into her head.
Suddenly an idea struck him. He began to
address the “ first curser ” in particular, and the
dupes in general in fluent Gaelic, the tongue of
his early youth. He hadn’t uttered more than a
sentence or two before the tohunga stopped, and
her followers began to look uneasy. They had
never heard anything like this before, and con-
cluded that it must be some newer and more
deadly form of “Makutu.” Finlay rattled his
words out twice as quick, and after another
moment’s hesitation the tohunga bolted for the
bush, with her party after her, and left the
relieved Hielander in possession of his life and
sundry tomahawks and fishing tackle. Another
little custom which the commission unearthed
was that of putting the names of children unborn
on certificates of title for land—a practical pro-
vision for Sir George Grey’s “unborn millions.”
Both male and female names were given to the
expected child so as to be prepared for all con-
tingencies because in these sort of cases you can
never tell, don’t you know !

In future London University is to be a
teaching as well as an examining institution.
The Privy Council suggests that its name be
altered to the “Albert University.” The Bul-
letin thinks that, considering the high status so
long enjoyed by medical and other degrees of
London University, graduates will not take very
kindly to this greasy proposal. “ London
University ” means something solid ; “ Albert
University ” carries with it gim-crack insignific-
ance.
Melbourne Standard reports: “There
is one good thing that may be said of a spider
there are no flies on it” We don’t know what
this means exactly, but we reprint it on the off-
chance that there is an obscure allusion to Parkes
in it somewhere.
The Government majority in the House of
Commons is 74. In the first year of the Parlia-
ment it was 118. The change has come about
through a Gladstonian gain of 17 seats at bye-
e'ections and the return of delinquents to the
Liberal ranks.
Election excitement and bush whisky have
done much for the Bega (N.S.W.) FiiEE Press.
Hear it on the recent Eden election contest : —
“ Duty calls as in the death-ride at Balaclava.
Silence reigns a moment as if invoking super-
natural courage. Death is defeated, the enemy
confronted, and victory crowns heroic honour.
From such a retrospect there is nothing to be
wondered at that Messrs. Garvan and Clarke
scored a two to one victory against the anointed
Goliath of the Parkes’ Government, clad in all
the gorgeous panoply of Government patronage,
and a forty-mile railway like a rainbow, in his
hand.”

“THE BULLETIN.”
(Published at SYDNEY, N.S.W.)
The Unique Weekly—Australia’s Nonpareil
Illustrated Newspaper.
“The Bulletin,”
Premier Australian Literary Journal,
Is the only paper which has a consistent and dis
tinctive Australian National Policy.
The Bulletin favours—
A Republican Form of Government.
Payment of Members.
One Person, one Vote.
State Revenue derived directly from the Land.
Complete Secularisation of State Education.
Reform of the Criminal Code and Prison
System.
A United Australia and Protection against
the World.
The Bulletin denounces —
Religious Interference in Politics.
Foreign Titles.
The Chinese.
Imperial Federation.
The public eye rejects as uninteresting more
than half of What appears in the newspapers of
the day. It is only the remainder that is found
in The Bulletin.
The Bulletin is an aggressive Democratic
paper which strives to exclude from its artisti-
cally-condensed columns all matter which is not
of general human interest. The Bulletin was
started eleven years ago with no capital but brains,
ana has become a vast property, because it pos-
sesses a vitality lacked by the countless news-
paper-ventures in which Australian capitalists
have unavailingly spent large fortunes.
The Bulletin’s conductors claim that every
progressive paper on the Australian continent has
more or less endeavoured to profit by its example
and follow its lines. The result of its powerful
influence and its unprecedented literary and com-
mercial success can at once be seen by comparing
the methods pursued by the Australian daily and
weekly press prior to and immediately after the
first issue of The Bulletin.
From the date of The Bulletin’s inaugural
publication its success was assured. It had at
once a dozen more or less servile, imitators, many
of which even took, so far as was possible, its
name. They are now all dead, while The Bul-
letin continues the one phenomenal success in
Australian journalism, the one completely non-
local and distinctly universal Australasian news-
paper.
The Bulletin proprietary have spent some
thousands of pounds in fitting their buildings
with the latest appliances for high-class printing
and the production of illustrations, and they have
now issued The Bulletin in an entirely new
form —printed with new type of improved legi-
bility on the finest paper, and adorned with
sketches from the hands of the ablest and best-
paid newspaper artists Australia has ever seen.
The Bulletin is the only well-established Aus-
tralian journal which, throughout its career, has
been consistently Radical. It points with pride
to the fact that for years it stood alone among
secular newspapers in advocating the Irish
National Cause, and that it was the only Aus-
tralian journal which denounced the Soudan
Expedition from its inception.
The Bulletin is the beacon-fire of national
progress; it is the forceful summons to the
crusade against the monopolies in land, wealth,
power, and privilege—the direct cause of half the
misery which now aiflicts British humanity.
Against the claims of avaricious clergy who claim
to monopolise salvation, against the dark despotism
of grasping plutocrats, and against the exercise of
any human authority which has not been granted
by the people over’ whom it is exercised, The
Bulletin will fearlessly and ruthlessly contend.
The Bulletin, unlike the ordinary weekly
journal, is not loaded with a weight of matter
transferred from the columns of a daily paper
issued from the same office. The Bulletin is
not connected with any other paper, and the
matter it contains is all specially prepared by its
own unequalled staff.
The Bulletin, which is the only paper sold
by runners in the streets of every Australasian
capital, is the most widely-circulated of all papers
published south of the equator, and is the only
Australasian publication which enjoys a popu-
larity unhampered by the political boundaries
that so closely restrict other journals to the
colonies in which they are printed.
The Bulletin’s red cover is equally familiar
to the. bushman of the Far North, the stockman
of Central Australia, the pearl-sheller of Torres
Straits, and the digger in the New Zealand
Ranges. A paper which is at once the most
popular city publication and the organ of the
intelligent bushman must indeed be broadly based
Perhaps the strongest proof of The Bulletin’s
popularity is found in the unchallengeable fact—
easily ascertainable from any postal official—that,
of all journals issued in Australasia, The Bul- .
letin is the one most frequently remailed from
one reader to another.
The Bulletin is the journal which, of all Aus-
tralian journals, is most frequently quoted in Eng-#
land, America, and the colonies.
The Bulletin is going ahead faster than any
other paper, because its vast mass of original and
specially-written reading matter is the brightest
and best, its illustrations the cleverest and most
artistic, and its policy the boldest and most
straightforward ever submitted to the Australian
public,

Noble-street, Newton, Geelong,
10th December, 1888.
To H. F. Kugelmann, Esq,
Dear Sir,—This is to certify that I, Patrick Keenan, was
taken ill about three years ago with a severe cold, which
turned into Bronchitis and became chronic. I consulted
more than one doctor, and during that time took a great
quantity of medicine without it doing me the slightest
good, but 1 became worse every day, wasting away until
1 became a perfect skeleton. About February last I began
to spit up blood, and what with the cough and severo-
pains in my left lung, and the quantity of bad matter I
was expectorating every day, I became very much alarmed.
Hearing of your professional skill I consulted you, and
you told me you could cure me for a given sum. 1 agreed
and went under your treatment in March last. At‘the
end of three months I was taken bad one morning, and
vomited about two pints of blood, matter and stringy
phlegm. From that time I commenced to improve, anil
was gaining in weight every day. About a month ago the
blood ceased coming, the cough disappeared, and the
lung healed completely, and thank God and your pro-
fessional skill, I am as well as ever.
P. KEENAN.
Declared and subscribed before me, one of Her Majesty’s
Justices of the Peace, for the Southern Bailiwick, in the
Couniy of Grant, this 10th day of December, 1888.
Peter Smytiir, J.P.
